CREDIT 
AND 
PROGRAM 
SUMMARY 
REPUBLIC 
OF 
BOLIVIA 
SOCIAL 
SAFETY 
NET 
STRUCTURAL ADJUSTEMENT 
CREDIT 
Borrower: 
Amount 
: 
Terms: 
Commitment 
Fee: 
Service 
Fee: 
Implementing 
Agency: 
Description: 
Republic 
of 
Bolivia 
SDR25.4 
million 
(US$35 
million 
equivalent) 
20 
years 
maturity 
and 
10 
years 
grace 
period 
A 
variable  rate  between 
0 
and 
0.5 
percent 
of 
the 
undisbursed 
credit 
balance 
set 
annually 
by 
the 
Executive 
Directors 
0.75 
percent 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
Bolivia 
has 
achieved 
remarkable 
progress 
in 
social 
indicators 
since 
the 
launch 
of 
its 
far 
reaching economic and 
social 
reforms 
of 
the 
early 
1990s. 
Those 
reforms, 
sustained 
and 
deepened 
in 
successive 
Governments 
up 
to 
the 
present, 
met 
with 
early 
success 
in 
stimulating  growth, 
but 
the 
economy’s  performance stalled after 
1998, 
due 
to 
several 
causes, 
and 
poverty 
increased 
over 
the 
last 
5 
years. 
This 
eroded 
popular 
support 
for 
the 
reforms, 
and 
recently 
led 
to 
a 
serious episode 
of 
civil 
unrest. 
The 
proposed 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural  Adjustment 
Credit, 
a 
two 
tranche 
credit 
intended 
to 
be completed 
by 
March 
2004, 
is 
a 
part 
of 
IDA’S 
immediate 
response 
package 
to 
the  current 
social 
and 
economic 
crisis 
in 
Bolivia. 
The 
credit 
supports the 
Govemment’s 
near 
term 
program 
of 
fiscal 
adjustment 
and 
provision 
of 
vital 
social 
safety 
net 
services. 
The 
credit 
thus 
will 
help 
the 
Government 
to 
close 
a 
financing 
gap 
in 
2003, 
expand 
the 
provision 
of 
social 
services 
during 
this 
period 
of 
fiscal 
adjustment, 
contain 
erosion 
in 
social outcomes 
for 
the 
poor, 
to 
address 
the  economic 
underpinnings 
of 
rising 
social 
tensions. 
Specifically, 
the 
program 
would: 
(i) 
protect 
the 
budgets 
of 
nine 
key 
programs 
(National 
Emergency 
Employment 
Program, 
Health 
Reform, 
Epidemiological 
Shield 
for 
1 
Page  8
Benefits: 
Chagas, 
Malaria, 
and 
Tuberculosis, 
Universal 
Health 
Insurance 
for 
Mother 
and 
Child 
Care, 
Expanded 
Immunization 
Program, 
Education 
Reform 
and 
Education 
Quality), 
that 
are 
subject 
to 
high 
volatility 
in 
investment 
budgets 
and 
expenditure 
execution 
and 
are 
key 
to 
the 
containment 
of 
further 
erosion 
of 
social 
outcomes 
and 
achievement 
of 
counter-cyclical 
goals 
of 
the 
2003 budget; 
(ii) 
sustain 
and, 
in 
some 
areas, 
strengthen 
key 
social sector 
reforms 
of 
this 
Government  and 
its 
predecessors; 
(iii) 
provide 
technical  support over 
the 
bridging 
period 
(June 
2003 
to 
March 
2004) 
to 
protected 
social 
safety 
net 
programs. 
The 
time 
horizon 
is 
a 
bridging 
period 
of 
9-10 
months, 
during 
which 
stability, 
both 
social  and  fiscal, 
can 
be 
restored, 
and 
the 
foundations 
for 
medium term reform 
put 
in 
place. 
The 
benefits 
of 
the 
proposed 
credit 
flow 
from 
its 
core 
objective 
to 
provide 
bridging 
support 
to 
the  Government 
through 
the 
difficult 
months 
ahead, 
while 
sustaining 
the 
momentum 
of 
social 
sector 
reforms 
during 
this 
period 
and 
protecting 
the 
substantial 
social 
gains  that 
Bolivia 
has 
achieved 
since the 
early 
1990s. 
The 
expected benefits 
by 
component 
are: 
Budget 
protection: 
41 
percent 
of 
the Government’s 
public 
social 
investment budget 
will 
be 
protected 
from 
reductions 
during 
the 
year, 
and 
execution 
of 
those  budgets 
will 
achieve a 
minimum 
level 
(85 
percent 
on 
average) 
by 
the 
end 
of 
the 
year; 
Public 
Employment: 
The Government’s  targeted 
workfare 
program, 
PLANE, 
will 
provide 
employment 
to 
unskilled, 
poor 
workers, 
mostly 
women, 
in 
projects 
that 
will 
maintain 
existing 
economic 
and 
social infrastructure; 
Health: 
Budget 
protection 
will 
be 
assured 
to 
public 
health 
programs 
benefiting 
poor families, 
while 
the 
efficiency 
and 
effectiveness 
of 
the 
health 
service 
delivery 
will 
be 
improved 
through 
performance 
agreements 
with 
regional 
health 
offices; 
Education: 
1.2 
million 
children 
will 
benefit 
from 
improved 
instruction, 
teacher 
training,  and 
school 
management 
as 
part 
of 
the 
ongoing 
Educational 
Reform 
Program 
protected 
under 
the 
credit;  options 
will 
be 
explored 
to 
address 
the 
declining 
enrollment 
rates; 
and  a 
final 
version 
of 
the 
Ministry’s 
sectoral 
strategy 
will 
be 
distributed 
for public 
.. 
11 
Page  9
Risks: 
Schedule 
o 
consultation, 
so 
as 
to 
strengthen donor  support 
for  key 
initiatives. 
Country 
strategy 
risks 
are 
substantial. 
The 
current 
Government enjoys 
limited 
support, 
and 
the 
coalition 
is 
fragile. 
Current social unrest 
and 
tensions 
could 
persist 
or 
intensify, 
further 
undermining 
the 
political 
environment 
for 
new 
medium 
term reforms 
in 
economic and social 
sectors, 
as 
well 
as 
economic 
growth. Balancing 
those 
country 
risks 
are 
the 
Government's 
efforts 
to 
deepen 
national 
dialogue, 
and 
hopeful 
signs 
of 
a 
regional 
economic 
revival 
that 
could 
help 
lift 
Bolivia's 
economy. 
Donors 
remain committed 
to 
support 
the 
Government, 
and 
there 
is 
a legacy 
of 
pro-poor 
reform  on  which 
to 
build 
an 
equitable 
development 
strategy. 
The 
risks 
associated 
with 
the 
credit 
and 
its 
implementation 
are 
manageable 
in 
regard 
to 
disbursement 
of 
the 
two 
tranches. 
Conditions 
for 
the 
disbursement 
of 
the 
first 
tranche were 
met 
prior 
to distribution 
of 
Board 
documents. 
Disbursement: 
SDRl8.1 
million 
(US$25 
million 
equivalent) 
immediately 
after 
loan 
effectiveness 
(expected 
in 
June 
2003) 
and 
SDR7.3 
million 
(US$lO 
million 
equivalent)  after 
meeting 
conditions 
of 
2nd 
tranche 
disbursement 
around 
March 
2004.

Number:

PROGRAM DOCUMENT 
I. 
INTRODUCTION 
AND 
STRATEGIC 
OVERVIEW 
A. 
Introduction 
1.1 
Bolivia 
has 
achieved remarkable 
progress 
in 
social 
indicators 
since the 
launch 
of 
its 
far 
reaching economic 
and 
social 
reforms 
of 
the 
early 
1990s. 
Those 
reforms, 
sustained 
and 
deepened 
in 
successive 
Governments 
up 
to 
the 
present, 
met 
with 
early 
success 
in 
stimulating 
growth, 
but 
the 
economy’s performance stalled after 
1998, 
due 
to 
several 
causes, 
and 
poverty 
increased over 
the 
last 
5 
years. 
This 
eroded 
popular 
support 
for 
the 
reforms, 
and 
recently 
helped precipitate a 
serious episode 
of 
civil 
unrest. 
1.2 
Bolivia’s 
major 
structural 
reforms 
since the 
late 
1980s 
are 
summarized 
in 
Annex 
8. 
There 
are 
many 
notable 
achievements, 
beginning 
with 
a 
bold 
and 
effective stabilization 
of 
the 
economy 
which 
brought hyper 
inflation 
under 
control 
in 
the 
late 
1980s. 
In 
the 
social 
sphere, 
1987 
marked 
the 
creation 
of 
the 
first 
social investment 
fund 
in 
the 
developing 
world, 
which 
created 
hundreds 
of 
thousands 
of 
man 
months 
of 
employment 
while building 
and 
reconstructing 
local 
transport 
and 
social 
infrastructure  nationwide. 
This 
was 
followed 
by 
a 
series 
of 
reforms 
that  opened 
the 
economy 
to 
foreign 
investment, 
improved 
education 
and 
health 
services, 
and modernized 
the 
customs  service, 
the 
judiciary, 
and 
the 
civil 
service. 
A 
major 
decentralization 
reform 
placed 
program 
administration 
and 
more 
revenues 
in 
the 
hands 
of 
municipal 
Governments. 
Bolivia 
introduced 
an 
ambitious 
reform 
of 
its 
formal 
pension 
system, 
and began 
the 
process 
of 
banking 
sector 
reform. 
Later, 
Bolivia 
would 
qualify 
for 
HIPC 
resources, 
and 
it 
pioneered 
the 
application 
of 
the 
Comprehensive 
Development Framework. 
Bolivia 
was 
one 
of 
the 
first 
generation 
of 
IDA 
countries 
to 
prepare 
a 
PRSP. 
1.3 
These 
and 
other 
achievements 
led 
to 
strong  performance 
in 
the 
social 
sectors 
which 
continued 
up 
to 
last 
year. 
Education 
enrollments 
and 
attendance 
increased steadily. 
Transition 
rates 
from 
primary 
to 
secondary 
school 
advanced 
(see 
Annex 
7). 
In 
the 
health 
sector, 
the 
child 
mortality 
rate declined, 
as 
the 
delivery 
of 
primary 
health 
services 
was 
extended 
to 
more 
and 
more 
of 
Bolivia’s 
poor 
families 
(see 
Annex 
6). 
Even 
in 
the 
more recent 
period 
of 
economic 
slow 
down 
and 
increasing poverty 
incidence, 
most 
social 
indicators continued 
to 
improve. 
Only 
education 
enrollment 
rates 
of 
the 
very 
poorest 
children 
showed 
a 
change 
in 
direction, 
declining 
in 
2001 
and 
2002 
(see 
Annex 
3). 
Protecting 
these 
substantial 
gains 
in 
social 
attainments and 
preventing 
any 
further 
erosion 
in 
school 
attendance 
are 
high 
priorities 
of 
the 
Government 
during 
the 
current 
economic 
and 
social crisis. 
1.4 
A 
central 
theme 
in 
the 
poor growth 
performance 
in 
recent 
years 
is 
Bolivia’s 
vulnerability 
to 
shocks-with 
attendant increases 
in 
poverty 
levels, 
deterioration 
of 
fiscal 
accounts 
and 
weaknesses 
in 
the 
financial 
sector. 
The 
slow 
progress 
in 
the pace 
of 
poverty 
reduction,  the 
continuing 
social 
exclusion 
of 
indigenous 
people, 
and 
the 
limited 
advances 
in 
reforms 
such 
as 
land 
tenure 
have 
resulted 
in 
rising 
social 
tensions. 
Land 
issues 
have 
increased 
in 
importance 
for 
rural 
indigenous 
groups 
as 
access 
to 
some 
of 
the 
traditional 
escape 
valves 
for 
dealing 
with 
rural 
poverty-work 
and 
1 
Page  12
remittances 
from 
Argentina, 
coca trade  and 
work 
in 
the 
cities 
has 
been 
curtailed 
due 
to 
the 
economic 
difficulties 
in 
Bolivia 
and 
Argentina. 
Armed conflict 
between 
the 
police 
and 
the 
army 
and 
civil 
groups 
in 
February 
marked 
a 
turning 
point 
in 
the 
deepening social 
and 
economic crisis, 
and 
convinced 
the Government 
to 
take 
urgent 
steps 
to 
address 
social 
issues 
more 
directly.’ 
1.5 
Bolivia 
and 
its 
Government 
are 
not without 
assets 
to 
bring 
to 
bear 
on 
its 
problems. 
Bolivia 
has 
long 
embraced 
pro-poor 
reform 
and 
modernization, 
and 
in 
the current 
crisis 
the 
Government 
is 
sustaining 
its 
reform 
focus. 
The 
country 
has 
promising medium-term 
prospects. 
Multilateral 
and 
bilateral 
agencies 
provide 
ample external 
resources 
(10 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
2002) 
that 
can 
be 
used 
to 
continue 
the 
ongoing 
social 
sector  and 
national reforms 
and 
to 
address 
more 
forcefully 
the 
obstacles 
to 
growth. 
It 
is 
not 
only 
the absolute 
amount 
of 
donor 
assistance 
that 
is 
important, 
but 
also the 
willingness 
of 
the 
international 
community 
to work 
together 
and 
make 
necessary changes 
to 
achieve 
results 
in 
the 
short-term. 
Moreover, 
the 
recent 
agreement 
reached 
with 
the 
IMF 
to 
increase 
the 
2003 
fiscal 
deficit 
target 
to 
6.5 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
provides 
an 
opportunity 
to 
make 
the 
type 
of 
gradual adjustment that 
could 
be 
politically 
and 
socially 
viable. 
These 
assets 
are 
now 
being 
engaged 
to 
defuse 
some 
of 
the 
growing 
impatience 
on 
the 
part 
of 
the 
public 
through 
national 
participatory dialogue 
on 
policy 
choices, 
a counter-cyclical 
budget, 
and 
a more 
vigorous safety 
net 
program. 
B. 
Causes 
of 
Bolivia’s 
Current 
Crisis 
1.6 
After 
nine 
years 
of 
rapid 
growth 
in 
the 
1990s, 
Bolivia 
has 
experienced 
a drop 
in 
real 
incomes and 
a 
decline 
in 
employment 
opportunities that have 
left 
the 
majority 
of 
the 
population 
feeling 
that 
17 
years 
of 
market-oriented reforms 
have 
not 
yielded 
the 
outcomes 
promised. 
Many 
Bolivians, according 
to 
opinion 
polls, 
also 
feel 
that, 
despite the sacrifices 
made 
to 
help 
stabilize 
the  economy, 
corruption 
and 
inequality 
have 
increased and there 
is 
the 
perception  that 
their 
demands 
for 
more 
Government 
activity 
to 
get 
the 
economy 
back 
on 
track have 
not 
been 
heard. 
1.7 
Uneven 
growth 
performance 
in 
Bolivia 
has 
fed 
those 
perceptions. 
During 
1962-1977, 
when 
the 
public 
sector  and 
the 
state 
mining 
enterprises  were  the 
engine 
for  growth 
and 
central 
to 
Bolivia’s 
development 
strategy, 
the 
Bolivian 
economy reached 
5 
percent 
to 
7 
percent 
in 
annual 
real 
GDP growth 
rates. 
Despite 
deep 
reforms 
since 
the 
mid-1980s 
that 
addressed 
a 
bankrupt 
state 
by 
placing 
the 
private 
sector 
at 
the 
center 
of 
Bolivia’s 
new 
development 
strategy, 
significant 
growth 
resumed 
only 
in 
the 
early 
1990s 
and 
then dropped again 
in 
1999 
and 
has 
been 
stalled 
up 
to 
the 
present 
. 
1.8 
As 
in 
previous 
decades, 
Bolivia’s growth 
of 
recent 
years 
has 
been 
associated 
with 
external 
factors, 
in 
particular 
large 
private 
and 
public capital 
inflows. 
Only 
a 
few 
sectors 
benefited 
from 
the 
‘ 
The 
preparation 
of 
the 
credit 
and 
the 
Government’s 
program 
has 
benefitted 
directly from prior 
reports 
on 
aspects 
of 
Bolivia’s 
social 
and 
economic development, 
including 
the 
Government’s 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy 
Paper 
and 
its 
latest 
revisions, 
and the 
following 
donor reports: 
World 
Bank, 
“Bolivia 
Poverty Diagnostic 
2000”, 
June 
28,2002 
(report 
no. 
20530-BO); 
World 
Bank 
Bolivia 
Country Office, 
“Bolivia 
Education 
Sector 
Briefing”, 
Feb. 
10, 
2003 
(mimeo); 
World 
Bank, 
“Poverty 
and 
Nutrition 
in 
Bolivia”, 
Dec. 
31,2002 
(report 
no. 
25581-BO); 
World 
Bank, 
“Bolivia 
Public 
Expenditure Review”, 
June 14, 
1999 
(report 
no. 
19232-BO); 
Caroline Moser 
and Oscar 
Antenanza, 
“Social 
Protection 
Policy 
and 
Practice 
in 
Bolivia” 
(Overseas 
Development 
Institute, Nov. 
200 
1); 
Inter-American 
Development Bank, 
“Bolivia 
Social 
Sector 
Program 
in 
Support 
of 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy”, November 
2001 
(report 
no. 
BO-0203). 
2 
Page  13
large 
FDI 
inflows 
of 
the 
1990s, 
notably 
hydrocarbons and telecommunications.  The 
soy 
sector, 
which 
prospered 
during 
this 
period, 
benefited 
from 
favorable 
trade 
agreements 
with 
the 
Andean 
pact countries. 
The 
rest 
of 
the 
economy, 
however, remained vulnerable 
with 
a 
small productive 
base 
and a 
weak 
private 
sector. 
Bolivia’s 
vulnerability 
became 
evident 
in 
the 
late 
1990s 
when 
a 
series 
of 
shocks 
(including 
the 
crises 
in 
Russia and 
Brazil 
and 
in 
2001 
Argentina) 
hit 
Bolivia, 
and 
the 
real 
GDP growth 
rate dropped 
from 
5.0 
percent 
in 
1998 
to 
less 
than 2 percent 
in 
2000 
and 
2.8 
percent 
in 
2002. 
Unfortunately, 
these 
external 
shocks 
coincided 
with 
a 
major 
push 
in 
coca 
eradication, 
an 
effort 
to 
reduce 
smuggling 
through 
a customs reform, 
and, 
later, 
pre-election 
uncertainties 
in 
2002. 
The 
net 
result 
of 
these 
external 
and 
internal 
shocks 
was 
an 
increase 
in 
poverty 
rates 
from 
62.6 
percent 
to 
64.3 
percent between 1999 
and 
2002. 
1.9 
The 
unfavorable 
situation 
in 
which Bolivia’s 
private 
sector 
finds 
itself 
can be 
attributed 
to 
several 
factors 
including: 
(i) 
stubborn 
vulnerabilities 
in 
the 
fiscal 
stance 
and 
financial 
sector 
reflected 
in 
high 
real lending 
interest 
rates; 
(ii) 
weak 
institutions; 
(iii) 
poor 
transport 
infrastructure; 
and 
(iv) 
lack 
of 
policy 
emphasis 
on 
export 
promotion. 
Indeed, 
recent 
growth 
studies 
on 
Bolivia 
point 
to 
external 
factors-such 
as 
external 
financing 
and terms 
of 
trade-and 
macroeconomic 
stability 
as 
key 
sources 
of 
growth, 
but 
they 
also 
highlight 
institutional 
factors 
such 
as 
the 
rule 
of 
law, property 
and 
creditors 
rights 
and 
widespread 
informality. 
By 
all 
measures, 
Bolivia 
ranks very 
poorly  on 
these 
institutional 
factors  that 
are 
direct 
hindrances 
to  private 
sector 
development. 
Private 
domestic investment 
had 
been 
low 
throughout most 
of 
the 
period 
prior to 
and 
after 
1998 
and 
was 
no 
more 
than 
US$54 
million 
(less 
than 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP) 
in 
2002. 
Furthermore, 
between 
1985 
and 
1998 
exports grew 
by 
only 
70 
percent, 
while 
other countries 
in 
Latin 
America 
increased 
their 
exports 
by 
2.5 
times, 
and 
other 
developing 
countries 
more 
than 
trebled their 
exports. 
1.10 
Thus, 
it 
is 
Bolivia’s 
high 
vulnerability 
to 
external 
shocks and the 
absence 
of 
incentives 
for 
the 
private 
sector 
to 
develop 
in 
areas 
other than 
primary 
sectors 
that 
is 
the 
root 
cause 
of 
the current 
economic  crisis. 
Moreover, 
increasing 
informality 
of 
the  economy 
has 
reduced 
revenues  and 
weakened the 
fiscal 
stance. 
1.11 
Despite 
the 
deterioration 
of 
Bolivia’s 
economic  situation,  social 
indicators  continue 
to 
improve. International 
donor support 
has 
helped 
Bolivia 
face development challenges since 
the 
mid-1980s 
(see 
Box 
1 
for 
a summary 
of 
Bank 
assistance 
since 1986). 
Support 
has 
remained 
strong, 
as 
successive 
Governments 
have 
adopted 
multisectoral 
approaches 
to poverty 
reduction, increased 
the 
sectoral 
emphasis 
on 
performance  and 
results,  and 
have  elevated transparency 
and 
civic 
dialogue 
to high 
levels 
of 
priority. 
3 
Page  14
Box 
1. 
World 
Bank Assistance 
since 
1986 
After 
a 
six-year 
interruption, 
the 
World 
Bank re-started 
its 
assistance 
to 
Bolivia 
in 
1986 
with 
support 
for 
the 
Government’s 
New 
Economic 
Policy. 
Over 
the 
next 
17 
years, 
Bank 
assistance 
increased 
with 
total 
disbursements 
reaching 
US$1.2 
billion 
over 
this 
period. 
The 
Bank’s 
early  (mid-1980s) 
strategy focused on 
restoring 
macroeconomic 
stability while 
helping 
to re-establish 
public 
and 
private productive activities 
(in 
mining, 
gas, 
and agro-based 
industries). 
During 
1986-1991, 
the 
Bank supported 
social 
protection 
programs--employment, income 
generation, 
and 
social 
assistance--aimed 
at 
attenuating 
the 
adverse 
effects 
of 
economic 
reforms. Bank 
assistance 
mobilized 
other external 
financing 
and 
supported 
Bolivia’s 
debt 
rescheduling. 
During 
the 
early 
1990s, 
IDA 
supported 
improvements 
in 
public 
sector 
financial 
management, tax 
reform 
implementation, 
restructuring public 
development 
banks,  and 
built 
the 
institutional 
structure 
for 
proper 
regulation 
(and 
supervision) 
of 
the 
financial 
system. 
Bank 
assistance 
during this period 
also 
focused 
on 
supporting social 
sector 
reforms. 
The 
transport 
sector also 
received 
important Bank 
assistance. 
During 
the 
mid-I990s, 
IDA 
organized 
a 
broad technical 
assistance 
program to 
support 
the 
capitalization 
of 
state-owned enterprises 
and 
to 
set 
up 
a 
proper regulatory 
framework. 
Support 
aimed 
at 
private 
sector 
development then started 
in 
1998 
due 
to 
recommendations 
made 
by 
OED. 
During 
the 
last 
few 
years, 
the 
Bank 
has 
helped 
the 
Government remove 
institutional 
weaknesses 
by 
supporting 
an 
institutional 
reform 
program 
and 
a 
decentralization 
process--while 
continuing 
to 
support 
health 
and 
education reforms, 
land administration 
and 
indigenous 
people development. 
The 
introduction 
of 
the 
CDF 
and the 
PRSP 
had 
important 
impacts 
on Bank 
assistance 
allowing 
for 
greater 
coordination 
of 
efforts 
among 
donors. 
Finally, 
throughout 
the 
current 
crisis, 
the 
Bank 
has 
supported 
the 
Government. 
Last 
year, the 
Bank disbursed 
almost 
twice 
as 
much 
as 
total private 
domestic 
investment. 
C. 
The 
Government’s Strategy 
1.12 
The 
Government 
aims 
to 
produce  rapid, 
visible 
economic  results 
to 
restore 
public 
confidence 
and 
to 
generate 
employment, 
while 
taking 
other 
steps 
to 
reduce 
social 
tensions. 
Their 
strategy 
has the 
following 
priorities: 
1.13 
Mitigating 
social tensions. 
To 
reduce 
social 
tensions, 
and 
with 
support 
from 
the 
Catholic 
Church 
and 
the 
Human 
Rights 
Assembly, 
the 
Government 
will 
work 
towards 
a 
National 
Agreement 
with 
political 
parties  and 
civil 
society 
organizations 
on 
sensitive 
issues  (coca 
production, 
salaries, 
land 
tenure, 
free 
trade 
agreements, 
natural 
gas 
policy, 
and 
the 
ruling 
economic 
model). 
A 
third 
National 
Dialogue 
is 
also 
planned 
for 
mid-2003 
in 
order 
to 
adjust 
Bolivia’s 
PRSP 
to 
give 
emphasis 
to 
the 
productive 
sector 
and 
the need 
for 
more efficiency 
in 
the 
delivery 
of 
health 
and education 
services. 
The 
results 
of 
these 
two 
processes 
will 
be 
inputs 
for 
a 
more 
comprehensive 
development 
strategy 
for 
the 
next 
few 
years. 
The 
Government intends 
to 
present 
this 
strategy 
to 
the 
donor 
community 
in 
September 
2003 
during 
the 
meeting 
of 
the 
Consultative 
Group. 
It 
will 
also 
introduce 
a results-based 
multi-year 
budget 
for 
the 
2004-2007 
period. 
1.14 
Strengthening 
the 
crisis 
response. 
An 
IMF 
stand-by 
agreement 
was agreed 
in 
April 
2003, 
and 
the 
National 
Congress 
approved 
the 2003 
budget 
soon 
thereafter. 
Favorable conditions 
under 
the  stand-by 
agreement 
allow 
for 
gradual 
fiscal 
adjustment. 
The 
budget  includes 
a 
10 
percent 
reduction 
in 
total 
current 
(and 
other non-prioritized) 
expenditures 
and 
a 
10 
percent 
increase 
in 
public 
investment 
to 
be 
re-allocated 
to 
the 
Obras 
con 
Empleo 
plan 
(short-term 
public 
sector 
programs 
in 
activities 
such 
as 
road 
construction, 
irrigation, 
rural 
electrification, 
public 
housing, 
and 
basic 
sanitation). 
Tax-raising 
measures 
such 
as 
restructuring 
the 
petroleum 
sector 
tax 
base 
and 
adjusting 
the 
tax 
law 
are 
included. Immediate productive 
sector 
actions 
are 
being 
identified to 
promote 
textile 
and 
wood 
exports. 
To 
mitigate 
the 
social 
impacts 
of 
the economic  slowdown, 
social 
safety 
net 
mechanisms 
have 
been 
put 
in 
place 
by 
continuing 
the  temporary 
emergency 
4 
Page  15
employment 
program 
(PLANE) 
and 
reinstating bonosol 
retirement 
payments 
for 
those 
over 
age 
65. 
The  Government 
is 
actively 
considering  demand-side 
financing 
to 
reverse recent  declines 
in 
primary 
school 
enrollment 
and attendance 
among 
the 
poorest families,  and 
will

special 
travel 
allowances 
for 
the 
poor 
to visit 
remote 
health facilities, 
among 
other 
initiatives 
(see

111). 
1.15 
Maintaining 
and 
enhancing confidence 
in 
the 
banking 
sector 
is 
a 
priority. 
Favorable 
conditions 
have 
been 
set 
for 
loan 
portfolio 
rescheduling, the 
loan prevision 
requirements 
have 
been 
reduced, 
and  the 
groundwork 
for 
increased 
banking 
sector 
efficiency 
has 
been 
prepared. 
A 
corporate  restructuring 
regime 
will 
be 
put 
in 
place and proper 
insolvency 
procedures 
will 
be 
established. 
Other 
initiatives 
are 
expected 
in 
the near 
future to 
re-program uncollected 
taxes 
and 
social security payments 
(for 
companies 
that 
defaulted before 
September 
2003), 
protect 
minority 
shareholder 
rights, and 
allow 
for 
the 
use 
of 
collateral 
other than 
real 
state. 
Also, 
the 
Central 
Bank 
is 
clarifying 
the basis 
for 
its 
liquidity 
support 
for 
and 
regulation 
of 
insolvent 
banks. 
1.16 
Foundations 
for 
medium-term, pro-poor 
growth. 
The 
Government‘s 
medium-term 
strategy 
aims 
at 
generating  pro-poor, 
sustained 
growth  while 
restoring 
a 
sustainable 
path 
for 
fiscal 
accounts. 
A 
performance 
agreement 
approach 
is 
being 
designed 
within 
the PRSP 
framework 
to 
reach 
measurable 
indicators 
in 
14 
productive 
chains. 
This 
approach 
will 
also 
be 
used 
to 
improve 
the 
delivery 
of 
social 
services 
mainly 
in 
areas 
related 
to 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs), 
such 
as 
maternal 
and 
infant 
mortality 
and 
primary 
education. 
Financing 
mechanisms 
to 
carry out 
this 
results-based approach 
are 
also 
being 
developed. The Government 
has 
announced a 
second 
stage 
of 
the 
Popular 
Participation 
program to 
enhance 
participation 
in 
the 
policy-making 
process 
while 
providing 
municipalities 
with 
greater 
fiscal 
autonomy 
and 
enhanced 
accountability 
mechanisms. 
New 
initiatives 
to 
deal 
with 
corruption 
are 
also 
expected 
including police 
reform. 
However, 
the 
Government’s 
weak 
political 
position 
poses 
an obstacle 
to 
carrying 
out 
a 
new 
gas 
export 
project, 
which would 
generate 
substantial 
revenues 
for 
the 
state.

to

E. 
Country 
Risks 
1.21 
The 
political, 
economic, and 
social 
situation 
is 
volatile, 
and 
there 
is 
a 
risk 
that an 
unexpected event 
could 
send 
Bolivia 
into 
a 
downward 
spiral. 
Also, 
factions 
within 
the 
opposition 
could 
try 
to 
force 
a Government 
change 
before 
elections. There 
is 
also 
a 
risk 
that 
the 
time 
needed 
to 
address 
problems 
will 
be 
absorbed 
by 
disagreements 
within 
the 
governing 
coalition 
and 
by 
responses 
to 
further 
rounds 
of 
protest 
or 
unrest. 
The 
risks 
increase 
if 
the 
economic situation 
deteriorates and 
no 
adequate 
social safety net 
is 
in 
place. 
The 
strategy 
thus 
assumes 
that 
the 
costs 
of 
helping 
to 
avert a 
deeper 
crisis 
by 
taking 
actions 
now, 
within 
a concerted 
donor effort, 
will 
be 
substantially 
lower 
than 
the cost 
of 
helping 
Bolivia 
recover 
from 
a 
steep 
decline. 
1.22 
responsiveness 
over 
the 
bridging 
period. 
While 
country 
risks 
are 
elevated, 
Bolivia’s 
assets, 
as 
noted 
earlier, 
are 
substantial. 
A 
rebound 
in 
Argentina 
and 
Brazil, 
an 
increasingly 
hopeful 
scenario, 
will 
generate 
jobs 
and 
exports 
for 
Bolivia. 
The 
Government 
is 
working 
closely 
with 
donors 
to 
finance 
an 
expansion 
of 
safety 
net 
services 
and 
pro-poor 
investments, 
while 
intensifying 
its 
The 
most 
effective 
approach 
for 
the 
Bank 
to 
address 
those 
country 
risks 
is 
flexibility 
and 
6 
Page  17
program 
of 
national 
dialogue. 
Risks 
specific 
to 
the 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural Adjustment Credit 
are 
presented 
in 
Section 
V. 
11. 
RATIONALE 
AND 
FRAMEWORK FOR 
THE 
PROPOSED 
CREDIT 
A. 
Rationale 
2.1 
The 
most 
telling 
measures 
of 
Bolivia’s 
current 
crisis 
are 
in 
the 
persistence 
of 
negative 
per 
capita 
income 
growth, especially 
in 
urban 
areas, 
high 
and increasing 
levels 
of 
unemployment 
and 
underemployment, 
and 
increases 
in 
poverty 
incidence 
(see 
Annex 
3). 
2.2 
Given 
the 
accumulation 
of 
effects over 
several 
years, 
the 
social impacts 
of 
the 
crisis 
in 
Bolivia 
can be 
expected 
to 
move 
beyond 
short 
term coping 
and 
into 
more 
structural 
responses at 
the 
household level, 
such 
as 
a 
reduction 
in 
the 
use 
of 
more 
costly social 
services 
or 
engaging 
the 
whole family, 
including 
children, 
in 
the 
search 
for 
income. 
2.3 
To 
date, 
preliminary 
evidence 
is 
showing 
that there 
is 
modest 
deterioration 
in 
primary 
school 
enrollment 
and 
attendance 
among 
the 
poorest 
families, 
within 
an 
overall 
pattern 
of 
improving 
enrollment  and 
attendance 
performance, 
between 
2000  and 
2002. 
The 
poorest 
20 
percent 
of 
Bolivian 
families 
are 
apparently 
losing 
hard 
won 
gains 
in 
enrollment 
achieved 
during 
the 
1990s. 
About 
4,200 
did 
not 
enroll 
in 
2001 
and 
as 
many 
as 
9,400 
may 
have 
left 
school 
during 
the 
year. 
If 
current 
trends 
continue, 
more poor 
children 
will 
drop out 
of 
school 
each year. 
This 
may 
be 
linked 
to 
sharp 
declines 
in 
average 
incomes among the 
lowest 
deciles 
in 
rural 
areas 
over 
the 
same 
period. 
Similarly, 
there 
is 
some 
limited, 
recent 
evidence 
of 
poor 
families 
cashing 
in 
their 
assets 
compared 
to 
earlier 
years 
(see 
Annex 
3). 
However, 
due 
to 
the 
substantial 
reform 
and 
public 
expenditure 
efforts  over 
the 
past 
10 
years, 
most  social  indicators, 
such 
as 
child 
mortality, 
communicable 
disease 
incidence, 
and 
primary 
completion 
rates, 
have 
improved 
through 
2001. 
2.4 
Equally 
important 
are 
the 
potential 
effects 
of 
the 
social crisis 
on 
the 
momentum 
for 
reform 
and 
on 
the 
social 
consensus 
regarding 
its 
benefits. 
Sustaining the 
reform 
momentum 
under 
such 
conditions 
and 
maintaining 
expenditure 
levels 
for 
key 
social 
safety 
net 
programs 
are 
challenges the 
Government 
has  embraced, 
and 
for 
which 
it 
is 
seeking 
financial 
support. 
2.5 
Budget 
management 
under 
present circumstances 
will 
be 
an 
important 
determinant 
of 
the 
Government’s 
success 
in 
managing 
the 
crisis 
and 
in 
communicating 
its 
priorities 
to 
social 
constituencies 
in 
distress. 
Key 
social  investment 
programs, 
more 
volatile 
in 
their 
budgeted 
amounts 
historically, 
must 
be 
protected. 
End 
of 
budget year 
pressures 
to 
reallocate social 
program 
budgets 
to 
other urgent programs 
must 
be 
contained. 
Counter-cyclical 
social budget 
allocations,  a 
prominent 
feature 
of 
the 
revised 
2003 
public 
investment 
budget, 
need 
to 
be 
protected, 
and 
their 
effective 
expenditure 
though 
the 
end 
of 
the 
fiscal 
year guaranteed. 
In 
this 
way, 
the Government 
intends 
to 
demonstrate 
its 
continuing commitment 
to 
pro-poor 
budgets 
and 
expenditure, 
while 
sustaining 
key 
social reforms, 
such 
as 
the 
temporary  emergency 
workfare program 
PLANE, 
the 
Mother 
and 
Child 
Insurance 
Program 
(SUMI), 
and 
investments 
in 
educational 
reform 
and 
infrastructure. 
7 
Page  18
2.6 
The efficiency 
and effectiveness 
of 
Bolivia’s 
social 
sector 
programs have been 
improving 
since the  1990s, 
by 
virtue 
of 
sustained 
reform 
efforts 
in 
health 
and education, 
the 
increasing 
local 
accountability 
for 
services, 
and 
the 
growing 
voice  and 
empowerment 
of 
the 
poor. 
These 
improvements 
can 
be 
seen 
in 
the 
outcome 
and 
impact 
indicators 
that 
have 
shown 
such 
sustained 
improvement  over 
the 
last 
10 
years. 
These 
improvements 
have  been  supported 
by 
substantial 
increases 
in 
public 
expenditure 
in 
the 
social 
sectors, 
both 
in 
real 
terms 
and 
in 
terms 
of 
proportion 
of 
the 
overall 
budget. 
To 
better 
measure 
and 
assess 
these 
gains, the 
Government 
in 
collaboration 
with 
the 
Bank 
and 
IDB 
is 
preparing 
a 
public 
expenditure 
review 
that 
will 
address 
in 
greater 
depth 
questions 
regarding 
the 
efficiency 
and 
effectiveness 
of 
public 
expenditures. 
2.7 
There 
is 
little 
doubt 
on 
the 
magnitude 
of 
the 
advance 
in 
social indicators. 
Child 
mortality 
has 
been 
halved 
since 1989. 
The 
health 
reform 
in 
the 
last 
years 
has 
produced significant 
increases 
in 
coverage 
of 
priority 
health 
services 
such 
as 
attended 
deliveries 
(from 
25 percent 
in 
1994 
to 
54 
percent 
in 
2002) 
and immunizations (DPT3 
going 
up 
from 
78 
percent 
in 
1994 
to 
100 
percent 
in 
2002), 
thanks 
to 
new 
policies 
such 
as 
the 
public 
insurance 
schemes, 
performance 
agreements 
between 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
and 
its  local 
health 
service 
offices, 
and 
the 
distribution 
of 
additional 
human 
resources 
mainly 
in 
rural 
areas 
(see 
Annex 
6). 
2.8 
Educational 
reform 
has 
improved 
the 
quality 
of 
primary 
education  and 
increased 
pre- 
primary 
attendance, 
by 
changing 
primary 
curricula, 
restructuring 
of 
the 
national 
educational 
system, 
strengthening 
of 
central 
administration 
and 
decentralization 
of 
management 
to 
municipalities, 
and 
by 
expanding 
community participation 
in 
the 
educational 
process. 
The 
gross 
coverage 
of 
public 
primary 
education, 
that 
in 
the 
early 
90s 
ranged 
from 
60 
percent 
in 
rural 
areas 
to 
87 
percent 
in 
urban 
areas, 
has 
reached 
100 
percent 
nationally. 
The 
internal 
efficiency 
of 
the 
public 
school system 
has 
also 
improved 
considerably; between 
1997 
to 
2000, the average 
number 
of 
years 
required 
to 
complete 
grade 5 
has 
been reduced 
from 
11 
years 
to 
7 
years 
(see 
Annex 
7). 
2.9 
These 
achievements 
attest 
to 
the 
underlying 
effectiveness 
of 
reforms 
and expenditures 
in 
the 
social 
sectors. 
Certainly, 
there 
is 
substantial 
scope 
for 
improvement over 
the 
medium 
term, 
but 
the 
proposed 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural 
Adjustment  Credit 
is 
motivated 
in 
part 
by 
Bolivia’s 
strong outcome  performance 
in 
the 
social 
sectors, 
the 
improvements 
in 
efficiency  and 
program 
effectiveness 
already attained 
in 
these sectors, 
and 
the 
opportunity 
to 
support 
key 
reform 
programs, 
and 
in 
some 
areas 
strengthen 
them,  at  a 
time 
of 
heightened 
risk 
to 
their  performance 
and 
financing. 
2.10 
The 
international 
cooperation 
community 
has 
been 
active 
in 
putting 
together 
elements 
of 
the 
fiscal 
and 
social 
packages 
in 
support 
of 
the 
Government. 
An 
IMF 
stand-by 
agreement 
is 
in 
place, 
and 
bilateral 
donors have 
committed 
resources 
to 
budget support 
and 
safety 
net 
activities. 
The 
proposed 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit 
is 
an 
integral 
part 
of 
this 
broader 
effort. 
2.11 
The 
proposed 
credit 
therefore helps 
the 
Government 
establish 
a 
bridge 
through 
the 
current 
crisis. 
The 
Government 
will 
be 
supported 
in 
its 
efforts 
to 
stabilize 
its 
fiscal 
situation,  strengthen 
safety 
net 
and 
pro-poor  social 
programs, 
and  signal 
the 
continuation 
of 
its 
existing 
reforms. 
Through 
these 
efforts 
the 
Government 
hopes 
to 
defuse 
social 
tensions, 
and 
to 
contain 
any 
further 
erosion 
in 
social indicators. 
The time horizon 
is 
near 
term, 
over 
a 
transition 
period 
of 
9-10 
months, 
during 
which 
stability, 
both 
social and 
fiscal, 
can 
be 
restored, 
and 
the 
foundation 
for 
a 
resumption 
8 
Page  19
of 
medium 
term 
reform 
efforts put 
in 
place. 
The 
Bank 
will 
be 
engaged 
over 
the 
period 
through 
intensive 
supervision 
activities 
of 
social safety net programs 
and 
of 
budget 
management. 
B. 
Social 
Risk 
Management 
Framework 
2.12 
As 
a 
low-income 
country 
with 
high 
poverty 
incidence, 
Bolivia 
faces 
fiscal 
constraints 
in 
its 
management 
of 
social 
risk 
and 
its 
specific 
safety 
net programs. 
Within 
the range 
of 
risks 
that 
poor 
Bolivian 
families 
face, 
this 
Government and 
its 
predecessors 
have focused 
their 
efforts 
on four 
areas. 
The discussion 
below 
summarizes  the 
evidence 
on 
social 
risks 
in 
each and 
the 
arguments 
for 
inclusion 
of 
different 
programs 
within 
the 
budget 
protection 
component 
of 
the 
proposed credit. 
2.13 
Health 
care 
risks 
for 
mothers  and 
children 
have  received 
increasing  attention 
within 
Bolivia’s 
health policies. 
Risks 
of 
premature 
mortality 
due 
to 
preventable 
childhood 
diseases 
and 
complications 
from 
childbirth 
are 
the 
focal point 
of 
both 
the 
maternal 
and 
infant 
health 
insurance 
program 
(SUMI), 
the 
Epidemiologic  Shield 
Program, 
the 
Health 
Reform 
Program, 
and the 
Expanded Zmmunization 
Program 
(PAI). 
These 
programs 
have 
helped 
improve 
both 
child 
and 
maternal 
mortality 
rates 
in 
Bolivia. 
2.14 
The 
risk 
of 
non 
attendance  at 
primary 
school 
level 
is 
a 
core 
consideration 
in 
social 
protection 
policies. 
Primary 
school 
enrollment 
and 
attendance 
risks 
in 
Bolivia 
have been 
the 
focal 
point 
of 
education 
policy 
since the 
launch 
of 
the 
reform 
era 
in 
the 
early 
1990s, 
with 
impressive 
results. 
Net primary 
enrollment 
and attendance 
levels 
have shown improvements, even 
during 
the 
crisis 
period, 
rising 
to 
88 
percent overall. 
(see 
Annex 
3). 
However, 
families 
in 
the 
poorest 
quintile 
are 
experiencing a 
modest 
decline 
in 
both 
indicators, 
pointing 
to 
the 
accumulating 
effects 
of 
the 
prolonged 
economic downturn. 
2.15 
Higher 
risk 
of 
unemployment 
or 
severe 
underemployment 
for 
working-age 
poor 
has 
been 
the 
most 
pronounced 
impact 
of 
the 
prolonged 
slump 
in 
per 
capita 
incomes, 
primarily 
in 
the 
cities 
where most 
Bolivians 
live. 
As 
recent performance 
of 
the 
economy 
and 
its 
social impacts show 
(Annex 
3), 
unemployment 
has 
increased steadily 
since 
1999 
from 
6.1 percent and reached 
9.4 
percent 
in 
major 
urban 
areas 
in 
2001. 
Preliminary indications 
from 
the 
2002 
national 
household 
survey show a 
slight 
decrease 
in 
unemployment 
rate 
due 
to 
lower labor 
force 
participation 
and 
possibly 
last 
year’s 
emergency 
employment 
program (PLANE). 
2.16 
The 
risk 
of 
inadequate 
income 
in 
old 
age 
has 
been 
the 
subject 
of 
important  program 
reforms 
in 
the 
pension 
system, 
and 
elderly 
Bolivians 
also 
benefit 
from 
a 
parallel program 
of 
cash 
transfers, 
known 
as 
BONOSOL. 
Both 
the 
reformed contributory 
pension 
scheme 
(individual 
retirement 
accounts)  and  the 
cash 
transfer 
scheme, 
BONOSOL, 
are 
financed 
outside 
of 
the 
Government 
budget, and 
are 
thus 
beyond 
the 
scope 
of 
specific budget 
protection 
interventions. 
The combined 
pension 
system, 
including BONOSOL, 
the 
new funded 
scheme 
and the 
former pay- 
as-you-go 
scheme 
are 
being 
analyzed 
intensively 
by 
the 
Bank 
and 
IDB, 
to 
prepare 
for 
medium 
term 
reform 
discussions. 
9 
Page  20
111. 
THE 
PROPOSED 
CREDIT 
A. 
Objectives 
of 
the 
Credit 
3.1 
a 
two 
tranche 
credit 
intended 
to 
be 
completed 
by 
March 
2004, 
is 
a 
critical 
part 
of 
IDA’S 
immediate 
response package 
to 
the 
current social 
and 
economic  crisis 
in 
Bolivia. 
The 
credit 
supports 
the Government’s 
near 
term 
program 
of 
fiscal 
adjustment and 
provision 
of 
vital 
social 
safety 
net 
services. 
The credit 
thus 
will 
help 
the 
Government 
to 
close 
a 
financing 
gap 
in 
2003, 
to 
expand 
the 
provision 
of 
key 
social 
services 
during 
this 
period 
of 
fiscal 
adjustment, 
to 
contain erosion 
in 
social 
outcomes 
for 
the 
poor, 
and 
to 
address 
the 
economic underpinnings 
of 
rising 
social 
tensions. 
The 
proposed 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit, 
3.2 
The 
credit 
is 
structured 
to 
sustain 
and, 
where 
appropriate,  advance 
the 
major 
reform 
initiatives 
of 
this 
Government 
and 
its 
predecessors 
in 
the 
specific 
areas 
of 
emerging 
employment 
(workfare), 
decentralization 
and 
municipal 
development; 
mother 
and 
child 
insurance, 
and 
health 
program 
decentralization;  education 
reform 
and 
the 
expansion 
of 
primary 
education; 
and 
national 
budget 
management 
for 
increasing 
social 
sector 
expenditures 
and 
their counter-cyclical 
content. 
Specifically, 
the 
credit 
will: 
a) 
Protect 
the 
budgets 
of 
nine key 
programs 
in 
social 
protection 
(workfare),  health, 
and 
education, 
that 
are 
subject 
to 
high 
volatility 
in 
budgets 
and 
expenditure 
execution 
and 
are 
key to 
the 
containment 
of 
further 
erosion 
of 
social outcomes 
and 
achievement 
of 
counter-cyclical 
goals 
of 
the 
2003 
budget. 
The 
protected programs 
include 
the 
PLANE 
program 
of 
workfare; 
the 
Epidemiological 
Shield, 
Expanded Immunization, 
the 
Health 
Reform 
Program, 
and Maternal 
and 
Child 
Insurance 
in 
the 
health 
sector; 
and 
the 
Education 
Nucleus 
Subprojects 
and 
the 
Education 
Reform 
Program 
in 
the education 
sector. 
b) 
Sustain 
and, 
in 
some 
areas, 
strengthen 
key 
social sector 
reforms 
that 
are 
pro-poor 
and 
provide 
safety 
net 
services. 
These 
include: 
actions 
to 
enhance the 
performance 
of 
PLANE 
11; 
a 
new 
initiative 
at 
the 
Fondo Nacional 
de 
Inversi6n 
Productiva 
y 
Social 
(FPS) 
to 
develop 
a 
more 
agile 
and responsive 
employment  orientation 
in 
crisis 
periods 
in 
the 
social 
protection 
sector; 
the 
improvement 
of 
health 
service 
delivery 
performance 
at 
the 
departmental 
(regional) 
level; 
provision 
of 
travel 
reimbursements 
to 
the 
poor 
for 
visits 
to 
distant 
health facilities; 
a 
new 
legal framework 
to 
empower 
women 
and indigenous 
people; 
a 
new 
education 
sector  strategy  that 
will 
coalesce 
donor 
and 
country 
support 
for 
educational 
reform;  and 
an urgent 
analysis 
and options 
paper 
on 
the 
recent evidence that 
enrollment 
and 
attendance 
of 
children 
from 
the 
poorest 
20 
percent have 
been 
in 
decline 
over 
the 
last 
two 
years. 
c) 
Provide 
technical 
support over the 
bridging 
period 
(June 
2003 to 
March 
2004) 
in 
key 
social safety 
net 
areas 
so 
that such 
programs 
as 
PLANE 
and 
SUM1 
and 
new 
interventions 
in 
health 
and 
education 
(travel 
reimbursements 
and  school 
attendance 
interventions) 
benefit 
from 
international 
perspectives 
and 
advice, 
and 
that 
the budget 
protection 
purposes 
of 
the 
credit, 
through 
end-of-year 
execution, 
are 
well 
supported. 
10 
Page  21
B. 
Credit 
Issues 
and 
Actions 
3.3 
The 
proposed 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
SAC 
contains 
a 
set 
of 
actions  that 
are 
summarized 
in 
the 
Letter 
of 
Development 
Policy 
(Annex 
1) 
and the 
Matrix 
of 
Agreed 
Actions 
(Annex 
2). 
The 
agreed 
actions 
are 
separated 
into 
four 
component 
areas: 
Budget 
Process; 
Social Protection 
(Employment); 
Health; and 
Education. 
The 
first 
section 
on 
budget 
process 
contains general 
and specific 
budget 
protection 
actions. 
For 
each 
of 
the three 
sectoral 
areas, 
there 
are 
steps 
to 
sustain 
and 
in 
some 
cases 
strengthen 
key 
social 
sector 
reforms.  Annexes 
4, 
5, 
6 
and 
7 
contain more detailed 
discussion 
of 
each 
area. 
The 
component 
areas 
are 
briefly 
discussed 
in 
the subsections 
that 
follow. 
1. 
Budget 
Protection 
3.4 
The 
credit 
provides 
for 
the 
protection 
of 
2003 
budget levels 
for 
specific social 
programs, 
selected 
according 
to 
explicit 
criteria, 
as 
a 
condition 
of 
release 
of 
a 
first 
tranche 
of 
the 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Credit. 
These 
criteria allowed 
for 
the 
inclusion 
of 
nine 
separate 
programs, 
and 
the 
exclusion 
of 
several 
other 
candidate 
programs. The 
credit 
also 
sets 
minimum 
budget 
execution 
amounts 
for 
each 
program 
as 
a 
condition 
for 
release 
of 
a 
second 
tranche 
of 
the 
credit. 
Annex 
4 
sets 
out 
the 
full 
discussion 
and 
analysis 
of 
Bolivia’s 
budget 
processes 
and 
outcomes, 
and 
the 
criteria 
for 
selecting programs 
within 
the 
SAC, 
here 
summarized 
as 
follows: 
Existing 
programs 
with 
counter-cyclical 
performance potential; 
Programs 
with 
financial 
or 
in-kind 
benefits 
that protect incomes 
or 
develop 
human 
capital; 
Programs that 
demonstrate 
good targeting 
performance 
to 
the 
poor 
and 
vulnerable; 
Programs 
of 
sufficient 
scale 
within 
the 
national 
budget 
to 
reach 
significant 
numbers 
of 
beneficiaries; 
and 
Programs 
within 
the 
national 
budget, 
the 
execution 
of 
which 
can 
be 
readily 
verified. 
3.5 
The 
justification for 
protecting 
the 
nine 
programs 
is 
based 
on 
several 
considerations. 
An 
analysis 
of 
Bolivia’s 
budgeting 
and 
budget 
execution 
performance 
since 
1995 
(Annex 
4) 
shows 
that social 
sector budgets 
and 
execution 
are 
volatile, 
tend 
to 
be 
cut 
in 
periods 
of 
low 
revenues, 
and 
thus 
tend 
to 
vary positively 
(pro-cyclical 
pattern) 
with 
changes 
in 
per 
capita 
GNP. 
Moreover, 
in 
times 
of 
a 
fixed 
budget 
ceiling, 
such 
tendencies 
are 
accentuated. These 
patterns 
are 
in 
part 
due 
to 
rigidity within 
the 
overall public 
expenditure framework, 
which 
focuses 
the adjustment 
process 
on 
a 
few 
items 
of 
the budget 
containing 
local 
resources. 
These 
fall 
into 
the 
public 
investment 
category 
where  social  expenditures  occupy 
a 
prominent 
position. 
The 
nine 
selected 
programs  show 
historically 
higher 
than 
average 
levels 
of 
budget 
volatility.  Given 
these 
patterns, 
the 
risk 
of 
disruptions 
in 
social 
spending, even 
within 
an 
overall 
increase 
in 
public 
investment 
in 
the 
2003 
budget 
compared 
to 
last 
year, 
is 
a 
serious 
concern and 
has 
guided 
the 
design 
of 
the 
credit’s 
provisions 
for 
budget 
protection 
and 
minimum 
floors 
of 
budget execution. 
3.6 
The 
agreed 
budget 
protection 
amounts 
for 
each 
program 
(Table 
1 
below) 
constitute 
prior 
actions 
for 
the 
release 
of 
the 
first 
tranche 
of 
US$25.0 
million. 
Budget execution 
floors 
for 
each 
program 
also 
appear 
in 
the 
same 
table, 
as 
a 
percentage 
of 
the 
protected 
US 
dollar 
amount 
in 
column 
one. 
Arrangements 
for 
release 
of 
the 
second 
tranche 
in 
regard 
to 
budget 
execution contain 
some 
flexibility 
to 
allow for 
unexpected 
program 
contingencies. Therefore, 
aggregate 
execution 
for 
all 
nine 
programs 
must 
reach 
85 
percent 
of 
the 
aggregate 
protected amount 
(2 
percent 
less 
than the 
sum 
of 
each 
program 
floor), 
and 
seven 
of 
the 
nine 
protected programs 
must 
reach 
their 
individual 
11 
Page  22
execution 
floors. 
This 
design 
still 
provides 
incentives 
for  individual 
programs 
to 
reach 
their 
execution 
floors, 
while 
allowing, 
realistically, 
for 
minor 
shortfalls 
in 
two 
of 
the 
nine 
programs.

of

Employment 
and 
Workfare 
Programs 
3.7 
The 
National 
Plan 
for 
Emergency 
Einployment 
(PLANE) 
was 
established 
late 
in 
2001. 
In 
its 
iirst 
phase 
over 
the 
course 
of 
2002, 
this 
workfare  program 
disbursed 
US$26.0 
million 
while 
creating  over 
3 
70,OO 
person-months 
of 
employment 
that 
helped 
implement  over 
4,400 
basic 
maintenance 
projects, 
mostly 
with 
tertiary 
roads 
and 
streets. 
Over 
half 
the 
beneficiaries 
were 
~vomen, 
all 
unskilled 
workers received 
the 
same 
wage 
of 
approximately 
480 
Bolivianos 
per 
month 
12 
Page  23
(about 
US$60) 
which 
is 
just 
above 
the 
minimum 
wage 
but 
below 
average 
wages 
paid to 
the 
poorest 
two 
quintiles 
of 
unskilled 
workers, 
and 
which 
under 
PLANE 
I 
assured 
a strong 
pro-poor 
targeting 
performance. 
The targeting 
performance 
was 
analyzed 
as 
part 
of 
a recent 
evaluation 
of 
PLANE 
I. 
3.8 
The 
administrative 
structure 
of 
PLANE 
I 
in 
2002 
included 
an 
innovative 
system 
for 
contracting: 
(i)

The Government had

3. 
Health 
Services 
3.12 
Providing 
services 
for 
the 
health and 
well-being 
of 
the 
population 
is 
a 
high 
priority 
for 
the 
Government, 
especially 
services 
targeted 
at 
the 
poorest 
population 
living 
in 
remote 
areas. 
The 
Government 
has  selected 
for 
protection 
by 
the 
Social 
Safety 
Net  SAC 
only 
those 
programs 
considered 
crucial 
for 
attaining 
the 
following  policy 
goals: 
health 
sector 
reform 
(including 
Extensa), 
epidemiological 
shield, 
expanded 
immunization 
(PAI), 
and 
universal 
mother-child 
insurance 
(SUMI). 
These 
programs 
are 
aimed 
at 
poor 
beneficiaries 
and 
have been 
associated 
with 
consistent 
improvements 
in 
overall 
health 
sector 
outcomes. 
Attended 
deliveries, 
for 
example, 
have 
risen 
from 
25 
percent 
of 
total 
births 
in 
1994 
to 
54 
percent 
in 
2001. 
For 
a 
more detailed 
discussion 
of 
the 
sector 
and 
the 
content 
of 
the 
Health 
component 
within 
the 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
SAC, 
see 
Annex 
6. 
Table 
1 
above shows the 
budget 
protection and 
budget execution floors. 
3.13 
The 
protected programs 
in 
health display 
a 
strong 
reform 
and

Within

protected under

Credit, 
the

supports

Other activities

Reform 
Program 
are 
immunizations,

improvements,  and 
support 
to

programs. 
The Epidemiological 
Shield 
(escudo 
epidemio16gico7 
EE) 
will 
keep endemic 
diseases 
under 
control 
and 
strengthen 
the 
disease 
surveillance 
system 
especially 
for 
chagas, 
malaria, 
tuberculosis, 
dengue 
fever,

and

These  are 
the 
most

problems 
in 
the 
country, and 
sustaining 
prevention 
and 
control 
measures 
within 
such 
programs 
are 
a 
fundamental 
policy 
choice 
during 
times 
of 
fiscal 
stress. 
Universal mother-child 
health 
insurance 
(SUMI), 
created 
by 
Law 
No. 
2426 
in 
November 
2002, 
is 
national 
in 
coverage, 
non-contributory, 
and 
consistent 
with 
the 
provisions 
of 
the 
National 
Short 
Term 
Social 
Security 
System. 
SUMI 
protects 
women 
from 
the 
beginning 
of 
pregnancy 
until 
six 
months after 
delivery, and 
children 
from 
birth 
until 
their 
fifth 
birthday. 
Integral 
with 
the 
country’s 
poverty 
reduction 
strategy 
(EBRP) 
and 
crisis 
response 
(Plan 
Bolivia), 
SUMI 
will 
be 
protected under 
the 
proposed credit. 
The 
Extended 
Immunization 
Program 
(PAI) 
will 
reduce the 
incidence 
and 
mortality 
rates 
of 
diphtheria, 
whooping 
cough, 
tetanus, 
measles, 
tuberculosis 
and 
poliomyelitis, 
through 
immunization 
services 
targeted 
at 
children.

A

recent  evidence 
that

and

for 
the 
very 
poorest 
children 
have 
declined 
during 
the crisis, thus 
undermining 
a 
fundamental and 
hard

of 
the 
many 
years 
of

reform. 
So

are 
modest 
(3-4 
percent 
in

enrollments  and

between 2000 
and 
2002), 
but

the

of 
this 
impact 
on 
the 
poorest 
only. 
The remaining 
80 
percent 
of 
families 
have 
managed 
to 
sustain the 
trend 
of 
increasing enrollments  and 
attendance 
over 
the 
same 
period, 
thus

gap 
between 
the poorest 
and 
the 
better 
off.

social 
programs 
which 
require 
budget  protection; 
and 
(ii) 
other 
policy-related  activities 
which 
the 
Government 
would 
carry 
out 
during 
FY 
2003 
in 
order 
to 
maintain 
the 
momentum 
of 
reform 
in 
the 
social 
sectors. 
3.22 
The 
two 
tranche 
structure 
will 
permit 
a 
sustained engagement 
with 
the 
protected 
programs 
and 
target 
reforms over 
the 
bridging 
period. 
This 
would 
yield 
benefits 
both 
in 
terms 
of 
supervision 
quality 
and 
monitoring 
of 
results, 
while 
helping 
to 
build 
the 
foundation 
for 
more 
medium term 
policy 
dialogue 
within 
the 
PRSC 
and other 
operations. 
Moreover, 
the 
second 
tranche 
contains 
16 
Page  27
budget 
execution 
thresholds 
individually 
specified 
for 
each 
protected program. 
The 
second 
tranche 
provides 
an 
incentive 
to 
implement 
the 
programs 
at 
historically 
realistic 
levels 
of 
execution, 
while 
protecting 
the 
programs 
from 
late 
in 
the 
cycle 
budget reallocations. 
3.23 
The 
weighting 
of 
disbursements 
between 
the 
two 
tranches 
is 
front 
loaded 
both to 
match 
the 
relative  weighting 
of 
conditions 
and 
to provide 
substantial 
resources 
at 
the 
outset, 
so 
as 
to 
help 
the 
Government 
finance program implementation 
on  a 
timely 
basis. 
The  conditions 
in 
the 
first 
tranche (eleven 
in 
number) 
contain 
the 
more 
significant 
steps 
in 
support 
of 
the 
safety 
net. 
These 
include 
the 
passage 
of 
the 
budget, 
protection 
of 
social 
safety 
net programs 
within 
the 
budget, 
the 
allocation 
of 
additional 
funds 
(at 
high 
financial 
cost, 
pending 
new donor grant 
flows) 
to 
PLANE 
11, 
the 
start 
up 
of 
PLANE 
I1 
with 
improved 
procedures, 
criteria 
and 
transparency, 
the 
significant 
reform 
of 
health 
insurance 
SUMI, 
and 
the 
signing 
of 
nine 
health 
performance 
management 
agreements. 
Second tranche 
actions 
(six 
in 
number) 
aim 
to 
assure 
the 
completion 
of 
safety 
net 
activities 
protected 
in 
the 
budget 
and 
to 
ensure 
follow 
up 
on 
issues 
and 
reforms 
within 
education, 
health 
and 
workfare 
that were 
initiated 
as 
part 
of 
first 
tranche 
activities. 
3.24 
As 
regards 
budget 
resources, 
the 
Government 
has  agreed 
with 
the 
IMF 
on 
a budget 
financing 
framework 
for 
2003, 
which 
includes  substantial 
increases 
in 
the 
public 
investment 
budget. 
To 
finance 
the 
budget, 
the Government 
has 
identified 
resources 
from 
various domestic 
and 
foreign 
sources, 
including 
IDA. 
The 
timing 
of 
this 
support 
is 
particularly 
important, 
given 
the 
need 
to 
finance Government expenditures 
in 
the second 
half 
of 
the 
Bolivian 
fiscal 
year, 
as 
the 
momentum 
of 
commitments 
and 
disbursements 
accelerates 
into 
the 
last quarter. 
Any 
reduction 
in 
the 
expected 
pool 
of 
foreign financing 
would 
require 
cuts 
in 
the 2003 
budgeted expenditure, 
which 
given historical 
and 
structural 
factors, 
might 
likely 
occur 
in 
roads, 
health, 
or 
education. 
The 
second 
tranche 
of 
US$10 
million 
is 
considered the 
minimal 
and 
sufficient incentive 
to 
achieve 
the second 
tranche conditions. 
The 
Government 
has 
agreed separately 
with 
IDA 
that 
any request 
for 
extension 
of 
the 
closing 
date 
would 
involve 
substantial 
revision 
and 
updating 
to 
the 
second 
tranche 
conditions, 
thus 
establishing 
further 
incentives 
for 
timely 
compliance. 
D. 
Implementation 
Arrangements 
3.25 
The 
lead 
counterpart 
for 
the 
credit 
and 
monitoring 
of 
the 
safety 
net 
program 
is 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance, 
with 
day 
to 
day 
responsibility 
assigned 
to 
the 
Vice Minister for 
Public 
Investment 
and 
External 
Finance.  Sectoral counterparts 
in 
education, 
health 
and 
social 
protection 
have 
participated 
in 
preparation 
and 
constitute 
an 
informal 
steering group 
for 
the 
credit. 
With 
technical support 
from 
IDA, 
the 
Vice-Minister 
will 
coordinate the achievement 
of 
first 
and 
second 
tranche actions. 
A 
combined 
IDA 
transport 
and 
social 
protection 
team 
will 
supervise 
PLANE 
I1 
during 
its 
implementation 
and 
provide 
technical 
support 
to 
UNDP 
and 
DUF 
in 
the attainment 
of 
100 percent 
execution 
of 
its 
protected budget and 
in 
the 
design 
of 
future 
links 
with 
longer term 
transport 
reform 
objectives. 
Technical 
assistance 
will 
be 
provided 
to 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
and 
UDAPE 
to 
help 
analyze 
the 
need 
for 
interventions 
to 
address 
enrollment/attendance declines and 
related 
program 
initiatives. 
A 
Public 
Expenditure 
Review 
will 
be 
prepared 
in 
coming 
months, 
including 
a 
thorough 
analysis 
of 
BONOSOL 
and 
pension 
reform 
issues, 
as 
they 
relate 
to 
coverage 
and 
fiscal 
sustainability 
of 
the 
pension programs. 
These 
findings 
would 
be 
incorporated 
in 
the 
medium term 
reform 
agenda 
that 
would 
be 
considered 
by 
the Government 
later this 
year 
and 
early next. 
17 
Page  28
E. 
Bank 
and 
other 
donor 
coordination 
3.26 
In 
the 
preparation 
of 
the 
Social

Net 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit, 
donor

have 
helped 
to 
shape 
the

of 
the 
credit 
and 
to 
convince 
the 
Government 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
social 
safety 
net strengthening. 
This

has 
been 
most intense 
in 
regard 
to 
PLANE 
11, 
for which 
donor 
financial 
support 
is 
crucial.  The consultations, 
in 
the 
form 
of 
meetings 
and a 
half 
day 
workshop convened 
by 
PLANE 
and 
DUF, 
inspired 
the 
creation 
of 
a 
formal 
donor 
coordination 
mechanism 
for 
PLANE 
11, 
improved 
the 
writing 
of 
the 
technical 
implementation 
document, 
and 
led 
a 
to 
a broader evaluation 
of 
the 
program 
for 
2003 
and a 
second 
financial 
audit. 
IV. 
ENVIRONMENTAL 
AND 
SOCIAL 
IMPACT 
AND 
FIDUCIARY 
REQUIREMENTS 
A. 
Environmental 
Impact 
4.1 
This  Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural  Adjustment 
Credit 
has 
no 
direct 
impact 
on 
the 
environment. 
For 
the 
purposes 
of 
Operational 
Directive 
4.01, 
it 
does 
not 
have 
an 
environmental 
category 
rating 
(unrated) 
and 
is 
not 
subject 
to 
the 
requirements 
of 
the 
Bank’s 
Environmental 
Assessment 
or 
other 
Safeguard 
Policies. 
To 
ensure 
compliance 
with 
environmental 
and 
social 
safeguards, 
an 
environmental 
screening 
process 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
for 
sub-projects 
financed 
through 
FPS and 
PLANE 
programs protected 
by 
the 
proposed credit. 
4.2 
For 
the 
FPS 
funded 
sub-projects, 
a revised 
operational 
manual that incorporates screening 
criteria 
and procedures 
has 
already been submitted 
by 
the 
Government and 
has 
been 
reviewed 
and 
found 
satisfactory 
by 
the 
Bank. 
These 
procedures 
are 
also 
apply 
to 
education 
sector 
school 
construction 
protected 
by 
the 
proposed 
credit. 
Health 
programs 
protected 
by 
the 
credit 
adhere 
to 
IDA 
and 
other donor 
standards 
of 
environmental 
safety. 
4.3 
For 
the 
PLANE 
program, 
the 
majority 
of 
the 
projects financed 
are 
maintenance 
in 
nature 
and 
are 
considered 
low 
in 
complexity 
and 
not 
expected 
to 
pose 
significant 
risks 
for 
environment. 
However, 
to 
avoid 
any 
negative 
impact 
on 
environment 
(especially 
for 
rural 
projects 
on 
water, 
irrigation 
and 
roads) 
and 
to 
ensure 
full 
compliance 
with 
environmental 
safeguards, 
appropriate 
screening, 
preventiodmitigation 
measures 
will 
be 
included 
in 
the 
PLANE 
Operational 
Manual. 
The 
manual 
will 
offer 
guidance 
and 
basic technical 
information 
on 
different 
variables that 
should 
be 
taken 
into 
consideration 
for 
the 
design 
and 
implementation 
of 
different 
types 
of 
sub-projects. 
It 
will 
also 
include 
a screening mechanism 
to 
identify 
sub-projects that 
would 
need special 
attention 
in 
terms 
of 
safeguards 
requirements.  Submission 
of 
such a 
manual, 
satisfactory 
to 
the 
Bank, 
is 
a 
condition 
for 
first 
tranche 
release 
under 
the 
proposed 
credit. 
B. 
Social 
Impact 
4.4 
Bolivia 
has an 
enlightened 
approach 
to 
social 
protection 
matters 
in 
its 
interaction 
with 
the 
indigenous 
people. 
There 
are 
35 
ethnic  groups 
in 
Bolivia, 
comprising 
an 
estimated 
4.6 
million 
people 
(58 
percent 
of 
the 
total 
population). 
GOB 
policies 
on 
indigenous 
issues 
are 
contained 
in 
the 
Development 
with 
Identity 
document prepared 
by 
VAZPO 
(Vice 
Ministry 
of 
Indigenous 
Affairs). 
It 
18 
Page  29
states 
that 
the 
GOB 
will 
aim 
at 
improving 
living 
conditions 
of 
indigenous communities through 
economic 
progress, 
political 
participation 
and 
cultural 
assertion. 
The Government 
will 
ensure 
legal 
security 
of 
indigenous 
lands, 
further 
economically 
sustainable 
initiatives, 
sustain 
cultural 
values 
and 
provide 
for 
basic 
services. 
Main 
strategy thrusts 
include: 
(i) 
development 
of 
infrastructure 
and 
economic  opportunities; 
(ii) 
development 
of 
social 
services 
with 
equity 
considerations; 
(iii) 
political 
development 
and 
(iv) cultural 
development 
with 
dignity 
and 
inclusion. 
4.5 
Government strategy 
has 
been 
evolving 
since 
then, 
in 
many 
instances 
under 
pressure 
from 
indigenous 
groups. 
There 
has 
been 
significant 
IDA 
and 
donor 
support 
in 
institutional 
strengthening 
of 
indigenous 
groups, 
which 
is 
being 
undertaken 
within 
the 
context 
of 
the 
Law 
of 
Popular 
Participation 
(LPP)~. 
4.6 
The proposed 
credit 
supports 
two 
main 
programs 
through 
which 
it 
intends 
to improve 
the 
well 
being 
of 
the 
indigenous 
population. 
In 
the 
health 
sector, 
the 
credit 
supports 
the 
empowerment 
of 
indigenous 
women 
and 
their 
rights to 
appropriate 
and 
culturally 
sensitive 
health 
services. 
A 
draft 
Bill 
of 
Rights 
for 
Pregnant 
Women 
(with 
focus 
on 
indigenous 
women) 
will 
be 
approved 
by 
a 
ministerial resolution 
and a 
dissemination campaign 
will 
be 
carried 
out. 
In 
the 
social 
protection 
and 
employment 
creation 
area, 
the 
PLANE 
program, 
the 
second 
phase 
of 
which 
will 
be 
launched 
in 
2003, 
is 
expected 
to 
reach indigenous 
poor 
through 
its 
poverty 
targeting 
mechanism 
(PLANE 
I 
reached 
at 
least 
60,000 
different 
workers, 
half 
of 
whom 
were 
women, 
and  about  a 
third 
indigenous). 
In 
the 
education 
sector, 
the 
sector strategy 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
includes the 
Bilingual 
Education 
Program, 
which 
recognizes 
four major 
indigenous 
groups 
and 
offers 
bilingual 
instruction 
on 
native 
languages. 
C. 
Financial 
Management 
4.7 
Bolivia 
has 
demonstrated 
significant 
improvements 
in 
the 
financial 
management 
area 
since 
1999. 
SIGMA 
(the 
integrated 
financial 
management 
systems) 
have become operational 
in 
all 
central 
Government 
ministries 
and 
now 
facilitates 
reliable reporting 
of 
budget execution. 
Data 
on 
Government expenditures have 
been 
made available 
to 
the 
public, 
though 
the 
internet. 
During 
2002, 
SIGMA 
has 
also 
begun 
operating 
in 
some 
decentralized  entities, 
including 
FPS 
and 
the 
National 
Customs 
Service. 
4.8 
All 
these 
were 
achieved 
through 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
financial 
improvement 
action 
plan 
agreed 
by 
the 
Bank 
and the 
Government, 
following 
the 
Country 
Financial Accountability 
Assessment 
conducted 
in 
1999. 
Main 
items 
of 
this 
action 
plan 
were 
incorporated 
under 
the 
Benchmarks 
of 
the 
First 
Programmatic 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit (PSAC-I.) 
and 
included 
i) 
the 
unambiguous 
application 
of 
the Supreme 
Decree that 
related 
to 
SIGMA 
and 
ii) 
a 
commitment 
to 
achieve 
agreed 
minimum 
benchmarks 
in 
financial 
management 
in 
all 
Central 
Government 
ministries 
through 
the 
implementation 
of 
SIGMA. 
4.9 
A 
review 
of 
the 
advances 
against 
benchmarks 
set 
in 
the 
PSAC-I, 
conducted 
in 
December 
2001 
indicated 
that most 
of 
the 
actions 
have 
been 
satisfactorily 
implemented 
and started 
producing 
results 
on 
the 
ground. 
However 
the 
scope 
and 
operations 
of 
SIGMA 
still 
needs 
to 
be 
expanded 
to 
LPP 
was 
launched 
in 
1994 
and 
provided 
the 
mechanism 
for 
indigenous 
organizations 
to 
exercise 
their legal 
representation 
and equipped 
them 
with 
legal rights 
over 
public 
investment. 
19 
Page  30
the 
rest 
of 
the decentralized entities, 
regional 
(prefectures) 
and 
municipal 
level 
in 
order 
to 
attain 
full 
completion 
of 
one 
of 
the 
major 
recommendations 
of 
the 
CFAA. 
4.10 
The 
GOB 
has 
demonstrated 
its 
commitment 
to 
decentralization 
and 
popular 
participation 
in 
local 
governance, 
as 
guiding 
principles 
for 
the 
reform 
of 
the 
country’s 
public 
administration. 
An 
anti-corruption 
strategy 
and 
institutional 
reform 
(fundamentally 
linked 
to 
the 
anti-corruption 
battle) 
Efforts 
are 
being 
made  at 
clarifying 
roles, 
responsibilities 
and 
fiscal 
and 
financial 
relations between 
different 
levels 
of 
public 
administration 
(national, 
pre-fectural, 
and 
municipal). 
are 
high 
on 
the 
Government’s 
agenda. 
4.1 
1 
Notwithstanding 
significant 
progress 
to 
date, 
there 
is 
still 
need 
for 
improvement 
in 
the 
area 
of 
transparency 
in 
the 
use 
of 
public 
funds. 
A 
Country Financial Accountability 
Assessment 
is 
planned 
for 
FY 
04, 
the 
results 
of 
which 
are 
expected 
to 
contribute 
to 
Bolivia’s 
economic 
development 
by 
(1) 
identifying 
weaknesses 
in 
Public Financial 
Management 
Systems 
and 
Practices 
(2) 
making 
recommendations 
for 
improving 
efficiency, 
transparency  and 
accountability. 
Upon 
completion 
if 
the 
diagnostic work, specific relevant actions 
may 
be 
considered 
for 
inclusion 
in 
the 
Policy 
Matrices 
of 
future 
operations, 
as 
appropriate. 
D. 
Procurement 
4.12 
Bolivia 
has 
initiated 
a 
number 
of 
reforms 
in 
the 
procurement 
area 
to 
strengthen 
current 
legal 
and 
institutional 
procurement 
framework 
in 
a  manner 
consistent 
with 
international 
procurement 
standards 
and  practices. 
These 
reforms 
are 
intended 
to  improve 
transparency, 
effectiveness 
and 
efficiency 
of 
public 
procurement, 
reduce 
corruption 
in 
the system and 
provide 
sustainability 
to 
the 
implemented 
reforms. 
4.13 
The 
CAS 
Progress 
Report 
of 
May 
2001 
highlighted 
the 
improvements  achieved 
in 
the 
procurement 
area 
within 
the 
CDF 
framework 
(expedited 
project implementation through 
the 
use 
of 
new 
procurement tools 
such 
as 
procurement 
plans; 
simplified 
procurement 
schedules; 
customized 
local 
standard 
bidding 
documents, agency 
capacity 
assessment 
(ACAs) 
exercises), 
as 
well 
as 
the 
remaining 
weaknesses 
in 
the system 
(the 
limited 
institutional 
capacity-particularly 
at 
the 
local 
level 
due 
to 
lack 
of 
qualified 
procurement staff, 
integrated 
information 
systems 
and 
procurement 
mechanisms that 
would 
ensure adequate 
monitoring 
and 
supervision). 
4.14 
The 
reforms 
initiated 
since 
then have 
included 
“Harmonization 
of 
policies 
and 
procedures” 
as 
one 
of 
the 
key 
elements 
for 
enhanced 
procurement 
management. 
Within 
the 
action 
plan 
of 
the 
Country 
Procurement 
Assessment 
Report 
(CPAR) 
agreed 
with 
the 
Bank, 
the 
Government intends 
to 
pursue 
this 
objective 
through: 
(i) 
the 
preparation 
and enactment 
by 
Congress 
of 
a 
modern 
Procurement 
Law 
compatible 
with 
international 
standards 
and 
practices, 
including 
the 
implementation 
of 
a 
national 
regulatory 
agency 
and 
a 
Code 
of 
Ethics 
for public 
procurement staff3; 
(ii) 
the 
gradual 
implementation 
of 
an 
E-Government 
procurement 
system; 
and 
(iii) 
adoption 
of 
standardized 
procedures 
and 
local 
bidding 
documentation 
at 
all 
levels 
of 
Government. 
The 
government recently issued 
a Supreme 
Decree 
in 
the area 
of 
public 
procurement 
that 
improves 
upon 
the 
existing 
legal framework 
for 
procurement 
by 
the 
public 
sector 
and 
represents 
an 
important improvement 
in 
the 
framework 
of 
public 
sector 
contracting. 
20 
Page  31
4.15 
The 
harmonization 
process 
(and 
the 
anticipated  improvements 
on 
local 
procurement 
procedures 
and  performance) 
is 
expected to 
help 
the 
Government 
reduce 
overall 
transaction 
costs 
and encourage 
donors 
to 
harmonize 
their 
procedures 
around 
the 
Bolivian

This 
may 
also 
introduce 
greater 
incentives 
to 
reduce 
corruption 
in 
the 
public 
procurement 
area 
and 
in 
public 
sector 
in 
general. 
E. 
Disbursement Arrangements 
4.16 
Disbursement 
arrangements 
would 
follow 
the 
simplified 
procedures 
for 
SALS/ 
SECALs 
approved 
by 
the 
Board 
on 
February 
1, 
1996. 
The 
Borrower 
will 
open 
an 
account 
in 
Bolivia's 
Central 
Bank 
(Banco 
Central 
de 
Bolivia). 
Once the 
Bank 
formally 
notifies 
the 
borrower 
that 
a 
tranche 
is 
available 
for 
withdrawal, 
the 
borrower may 
submit 
a 
simplified 
withdrawal 
application 
so 
that the proceeds 
of 
the 
tranche 
are 
deposited 
by 
the 
Bank 
in 
this 
account, 
for 
use 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
Loan 
Agreement. Disbursements 
will 
not 
be 
linked 
to 
specific 
purchases, 
and 
supporting 
evidence 
for 
disbursements 
is 
therefore 
not 
required. 
The 
proceeds 
of 
the 
loan 
may not 
be 
used 
to 
finance expenditures 
typically 
excluded 
under 
the 
Loan 
Agreement. 
Although 
a 
routine 
audit 
of 
the 
deposit 
account 
would 
not be 
required, 
the 
Bank 
reserves 
the 
right 
to 
request 
that 
an 
audit 
be 
conducted 
in 
accordance 
with 
International 
Standards 
on 
Auditing, 
by 
an  independent 
firm 
acceptable 
to 
the 
Bank. 
If 
requested, 
the 
audit 
will 
be 
performed 
in 
accordance 
with 
terms 
of 
reference 
(TORS) 
approved 
by 
the 
Bank. 
V. 
BENEFITS 
AND 
RISKS 
5.1 
Bolivia 
and 
its 
people 
are 
struggling 
to 
emerge 
from 
a 
sustained 
Economic downturn 
that 
has 
left 
the 
poor 
and 
vulnerable 
segments 
of 
the 
economy worse 
off 
in 
a 
context 
of 
slow  or 
negative 
per 
capita 
growth 
since 
1998 
(see 
Annex 
3 
for 
changes 
in 
per 
capita 
income 
by 
decile 
and 
region). 
This 
persistent 
social crisis 
came 
to 
a head 
in 
February 
of 
this 
year 
with 
violent 
civil 
unrest, 
which 
in 
turn 
further 
depressed 
tax 
revenues 
and 
sparked 
continuing 
rounds 
of 
protest 
and 
social 
tensions. 
The benefits 
of 
the proposed 
credit 
flow from 
its 
core 
objective 
to 
provide 
a 
financial  bridge 
for 
the 
Government  through 
the 
difficult 
months 
ahead, 
while 
sustaining  the 
momentum 
of 
social 
sector 
reforms 
during 
this 
period 
and 
ensuring 
the 
counter-cyclical impact 
of 
key 
safety 
net programs 
for 
2003. 
This 
should 
help 
the Government 
to 
restore 
stability 
and 
address 
social 
tensions, 
key 
antecedents 
for 
a 
restoration 
of 
a 
stronger 
growth 
path 
and resumption 
of 
medium 
term 
reform 
efforts. 
Credit 
resources 
will 
contribute 
to 
the 
financing 
of 
the 
revised 
2003 
budget, 
within 
an 
overall fiscal framework 
agreed 
with 
the 
IMF 
and 
as 
part 
of 
a 
larger 
package 
of 
IDA 
and 
other donor support. 
5.2 
The 
social 
safety 
net 
in 
Bolivia 
is 
constrained 
by 
fiscal 
resources 
and 
the 
large 
size 
of 
the 
potential 
target 
group, the 
poor. 
The credit 
is 
designed, 
within 
these 
limitations, 
to 
strengthen and 
protect 
key 
programs and thereby 
to 
ensure 
an 
affordable 
flow 
of 
compensatory 
resources 
to 
the 
poorer 
segments 
of 
Bolivian 
society 
during 
this 
period.  Through 
the budget 
protection 
provision 
of 
the 
proposed 
credit 
and 
the agreed 
sustaining reforms, 
the 
proposed 
credit 
therefore 
seeks 
to 
support 
the 
Government 
in 
its 
efforts 
to 
address 
the 
intensifying 
social 
tensions, 
contain 
the 
worsening 
social 
indicators, 
including 
unemployment 
and the 
preliminary 
evidence 
of 
decreasing 
21 
Page  32
enrollments/attendance, 
expected 
benefits 
by 
component 
are 
: 
and

As

The current  Government

Opposition

Current

for

in

as 
well

A 
further

of 
the

a 
higher 
risk 
if 
IDA

could 
take

Balancing

encouraging 
signs 
of 
a

that 
could

economy.

Abundant

to

Budget

in

Actions

forward

Dear
