Wolfensohn, 
1. 
This 
document 
describes 
the 
groundwork 
of 
the 
Social Protection 
Network 
Support 
Program 
(Programa 
de 
Apoyo 
a 
la 
Red 
de 
Proteccih 
Social,  PARPS). 
It 
starts 
by 
presenting 
the 
macroeconomic and 
fiscal 
situation 
resulting 
from 
the crisis, characterized 
by 
a reduction 
in 
economic growth, increased 
unemployment 
and 
high 
poverty 
rates. 
It 
goes 
on 
to 
describe the 
content 
of 
the 
Social Protection 
Spending Program, support 
for 
counter-cyclical 
programs 
and 
the 
consolidation 
of 
social 
reforms 
to 
ensure the 
poor’s 
human  capital. 
These 
measures 
fall 
within 
the 
policies 
the 
Government 
has 
been 
implementing 
to 
ward 
off 
the 
crisis. 
Macroeconomic 
and 
fiscal 
situation 
2. 
At 
the 
close 
of 
2002, 
the 
Bolivian 
economy recorded 
a 
growth 
rate 
of 
2.75 
percent, 
cumulative 
inflation 
of 
2.45 
percent, 
a 
fiscal 
deficit 
of 
8.7 
percent 
of 
GDP, 
a 
net 
international 
reserves 
(NIR) 
loss 
by 
the 
Central 
Bank 
of 
Bolivia 
(BCB) 
of 
US$274.2 
million, 
and 
an 
increase 
in 
the 
money supply 
of 
Bs. 
337 
million. 
The 
year 
2002 
was 
the 
fourth 
consecutive 
year 
in 
which 
annual 
growth 
rates 
were 
less 
than 
three 
percent, 
a 
sign 
of 
severe 
contraction 
in 
economic 
activity. 
3. 
Consistent 
with 
the 
lethargy 
in 
the 
economy, 
significant 
increases 
have been observed 
in 
the 
rates 
of 
open unemployment, 
which 
reached 
9.5 
percent 
in 
2001 and 
8.5 
percent 
in 
2002. 
Similarly, 
the 
quality 
of 
employment 
deteriorated, 
with 
informal 
employment 
accounting 
for 
64 percent 
of 
the 
working 
population. 
The 
reduction 
in 
per capita 
income, 
in 
turn, 
was 
reflected 
in 
the 
poverty 
rate, 
which 
rested between 
63 
percent 
and 
65 
percent. 
4. 
The 
key 
elements 
in 
the 
crisis 
revolve 
around 
external 
effects-the 
international 
financial 
crisis,  and 
its 
effect 
on 
Brazil 
(1998) 
and 
later 
Argentina 
(late 
2001)-and 
internal 
ones-the 
reduction 
in 
the  coca 
trade  and 
its 
impact 
on 
the 
informal 
sector, 
24 
Page  35
among 
others. 
Both 
shocks 
led 
to 
real 
stagnation 
in 
the sector, weakened revenues 
and 
financial 
instability. 
5. 
The 
external 
shocks 
were 
primarily 
evidenced 
through 
the 
drop 
in 
external 
demand 
for

and

drop 
in

(FDI);

as

of 
the 
periods 
of

in

6. 
Among

of 
note 
that 
restricted

policies 
for

drug

generated

which 
dropped 
from 
2.8 
percent 
of

in

to 
1 
percent 
in 
2000. 
In

during 
the 
decade 
of 
the 
 OS, 
some 
US$200 
million 
in 
earnings 
were 
lost 
per

on 
average, 
which 
had 
served 
to 
hold 
up 
the 
informal 
sector's 
activity, 
and 
internal 
demand 
dropped 
due 
to 
the 
decreased 
surpluses 
from 
the 
economy 
of 
coca 
in 
the 
informal 
market. 
7. 
Among 
the 
major 
consequences 
of 
the 
contraction 
in 
the 
sector, 
we 
find 
the 
decline 
in 
financial 
intermediation 
activity 
due 
to 
the  sustained 
contraction 
in 
deposits, 
and 
a 
decline 
in 
portfolios 
reported 
by 
some 
private 
banks, 
leading 
to 
increased 
arrearage 
and 
causing other 
banks 
to 
adopt 
more 
prudent 
policies 
for

8. 
Also, 
starting 
in 
1999, 
the 
drop 
in 
economic 
activity 
and 
the 
cost 
of 
pension 
reform 
became 
largely 
responsible 
for 
the 
fiscal 
deficit 
(8.7 
percent 
of 
GDP). 
The 
increase 
in 
the 
fiscal  deficit 
in 
2002 
was  aggravated 
by

to 
be 
one 
of 
the poorest countries 
in 
the 
region 
despite 
important 
improvements 
in 
non-monetary 
poverty 
measures 
during 
the  1990s 
linked 
to 
increasing social expenditure 
and 
better 
delivery 
of 
social 
services. 
While 
the 
crisis 
has 
affected 
the 
population 
overall, 
there 
is 
suggestive evidence  that 
it 
has 
increased 
the 
vulnerability 
of 
the 
poor 
in 
particular, 
drawing down 
the 
little 
assets 
they 
accumulate and 
compromising 
their 
human 
capital 
development. 
TRENDS 
IN 
POVERTY INDICATORS 
2. 
Bolivia 
registered 
limited 
progress 
in 
reducing 
income poverty 
during 
the 1990s. 
Although 
nationally 
representative household 
surveys 
are 
lacking for 
the 
first 
half 
of 
the 
1990s, 
the 
available 
data 
indicates a notable 
reduction 
in 
poverty 
rates 
for 
large 
cities 
from 
52% 
in 
1993 
to 
47% 
in 
1999. 
There 
was 
a 
likely 
smaller 
decline 
in 
poverty 
in 
smaller  cities 
and 
rural 
areas 
during 
this 
period. 
This 
trend 
was 
reversed 
with 
the 
economic  stagnation that 
set 
in 
after 
1999. 
Although 
the  analysis 
of 
the 
most 
recent 
household survey 
data 
for 
2002 
is 
not 
yet 
finalized, 
preliminary 
evidence 
conforms 
to 
some 
worrisome 
trends 
observed 
during 
1999-2001. 
3. 
Poverty levels 
in 
Bolivia 
are 
higher 
today than 
at onset 
of 
the current 
crisis 
(see 
Table 
1). 
The 
short-lived 
decline 
in 
poverty 
rates 
in 
2001, 
that 
followed 
a 
significant 
increase 
from 
1999 
to 
2000, 
had 
lost 
momentum 
by 
2002. 
The 
percentage 
of 
the 
population 
living 
in 
poverty 
increased 
slightly 
from 
62.6% 
in 
1999 
to 
an 
estimated 64.3% 
by 
the 
end 
of 
2002 
while 
the 
percentage 
in 
extreme poverty, 
defined 
as 
an 
insufficient 
income 
to 
consume 
a 
minimum 
food 
basket, 
went 
from 
36% 
to 
around 
37% 
during 
this 
period. 
Although 
poverty 
continues 
to 
be 
higher 
in 
the 
rural 
areas, 
with 
about 82% 
of 
the 
population currently 
living 
in 
poverty, 
it 
deteriorated 
mostly 
in 
urban 
areas, 
from 
51.5% 
in 
1999 
to 
about 
53.6% 
in 
2002. 
Poverty 
continues 
to 
affect more 
the 
indigenous 
people 
and 
rural 
women, 
over 
80% 
are 
below 
the 
poverty 
line 
and 
nearly 
half 
are 
extremely 
poor.

Inequality 
remains among 
the 
highest 
in 
the 
region, 
with 
the 
poorest 
quintile 
holding 
only 
3% 
of 
total 
income 
compared 
to 
more 
than 
60% 
of 
the 
richest 
quintile. 
This 
high 
level 
of 
inequality 
has 
been 
a 
key 
reason 
why 
economic 
growth 
has 
made 
far 
less 
of 
a 
dent 
in 
poverty 
in 
Bolivia. 
Employment and 
labor 
incomes 
4. 
There 
is 
evidence that 
the 
crisis 
has 
taken a 
toll 
on 
the 
in 
the 
level 
and 
quality 
of 
employment 
of 
the 
poorest 
families. 
The 
open 
unemployment  rate 
in 
main 
cities 
increased 
steadily 
from 
6.1% 
in 
1999 
to 
9.4% 
in 
2001, 
especially among 
women, 
and 
came 
down 
slightly 
in 
2002 (Figure 
1). 
The 
latter probably 
results 
from 
discouraged 
workers 
leaving 
the 
labor force according 
to 
preliminary 
evidence (the 
participation 
rate 
declined 
from 
61% 
to 
57.4%). 
The 
rise 
in 
unemployment affects 
more 
workers 
from 
the 
poorest 
families 
whom 
are 
twice 
more 
likely 
to 
be 
unemployed than 
the 
average 
worker. 
It 
also 
takes 
longer 
for 
the 
low-income 
unemployed 
to 
find 
a 
job. 
In 
2002, 
60% 
of 
workers 
from 
the 
poorest 20% 
of 
families 
took 
at 
least 
two 
months 
to 
find 
a 
job, 
compared 
to 
40% 
in 
2000. 
An 
increasing 
fraction 
of 
workers 
cite 
factors related 
to 
the 
economic 
downturn 
(e.g., 
being 
fired, 
lack 
of 
sales 
or 
providers) 
as 
the 
main 
reasons 
for 
being 
unemployed 
during 
this 
period. 
Underemployment 
has 
also  become 
more 
prevalent. 
With 
informality 
still 
comprising 
over 
80% 
of 
jobs 
in 
the 
poorest 
quintiles, 
over 
one 
third 
of 
those 
employed 
in 
2002 
were 
willing 
and 
available 
to work 
longer 
hours, 
up 
from 
one 
fourth 
in 
2000.

1

evidence 
points 
to

in

and 
the 
assets 
of 
the 
poor. 
Real 
earnings have 
declined 
by 
over 
10% 
on 
average 
during 
1999-2002 
for

for

secondary. 
Real income per 
capita 
fell 
between 
2% 
and 
4% 
for 
urban 
households 
throughout 
the 
income 
distribution 
(Figure 
2). 
Meanwhile rural 
households 
in 
the 
bottom 
three 
income 
deciles 
saw 
their real 
percapita incomes 
decline 
while 
those 
in 
the 
upper 
deciles 
registered 
positive 
gains. 
The 
evidence  also 
suggests 
that 
the 
increase 
in 
vulnerability 
has 
led 
to 
an increasing 
number 
of 
poor 
households 
to draw 
down 
on 
family 
assets. 
The 
percentage 
of 
households 
among 
the 
poorest 
40% 
of 
families 
that 
report 
to 
have suffered 
a 
loss 
in 
both 
their 
regular 
income 
and 
assets 
have 
doubled 
from 
2000 
to 
2002.

Education 
and 
health 
6. 
There 
are 
increasing 
concerns that the 
strain 
imposed 
by 
the 
crisis 
may 
seriously 
compromise 
the  achievements 
in 
social indicators  and 
therefore 
the 
sustainability 
of 
poverty 
reduction 
and 
millennium 
development 
targets. 
Bolivia 
has 
achieved 
considerable 
improvements 
in 
living 
conditions 
during 
the 
1990s, 
as 
measured 
by 
the 
level 
of 
unsatisfied 
basic 
needs 
(see 
Table 
2). 
Both 
the 
urban 
and 
rural 
population 
with 
unsatisfied 
basic 
needs 
declined 
steadily 
from 
70.9% 
in 
1992 
to 
58.6% 
in 
2001, 
reflecting 
improved 
access 
to 
education, 
healthcare, 
housing 
and 
basic 
infrastructure 
in 
both 
urban 
and 
rural 
areas, 
although  significant 
disparities  persist 
between  municipalities. 
This 
results 
in 
part 
from 
a steady 
increase 
in 
social 
expenditure 
over 
the 
decade 
(in 
the 
last 
five 
years 
only 
from 
12.4 
percent 
of 
GDP 
in 
1995 
to 
18.5 
percent 
in 
2001), 
and 
has 
led to 
a 
significant 
improvement 
in 
education, 
health and 
other social indicators, although 
as 
shown 
above 
it 
has 
not 
translated 
in 
improvements 
in 
income 
poverty.

7. 
There 
is 
suggestive 
evidence  that 
as 
the 
incomes 
of 
the 
poor 
Bolivians 
have 
declined, 
they 
may 
have 
coped 
by 
withdrawing children 
from 
school. 
While 
both 
school 
enrollment 
and 
absenteeism 
figures 
continued 
to improve 
overall, 
they 
show 
a steady 
deterioration 
for 
children 
of 
the 
poorest 
families 
(see 
Table 
3). 
About 
80% 
of 
children 
aged 
5 
to 
12 
from 
the 
poorest 
20% 
of 
families 
enrolled 
in 
2002 
down 
from 
84% 
in 
2000. 
Moreover, 
of 
those 
enrolled 
in 
this 
group 
20% 
did 
not 
attend 
school regularly, 
up 
from 
16.5% 
in 
2000. 
In 
contrast, 
enrollment 
and 
attendance 
of 
children 
in 
the 
richest 
quintiles 
show 
a 
moderate 
increase. 
8. 
As 
for 
health 
service 
utilization, 
the  evidence 
does 
not 
indicate 
a 
systematic 
deterioration 
of 
the 
demand 
for 
maternal 
and 
infant 
medical 
attention. 
The 
usage 
of 
skilled 
medical 
personnel and 
health facilities, very 
low 
compared 
to 
other 
poor 
countries 
in 
the 
region, 
had 
increased 
during 
the 
1990s 
but 
has 
slowed 
down 
in 
recent 
years. 
About 
54% 
of 
the 
episodes 
of 
diarrhea 
of 
children 
in 
the 
poorest 
families 
in 
2002 
did 
not 
receive 
consultation 
from 
skilled 
personnel, 
similar 
to 
2000. 
For 
the 
case 
of 
IRAs 
this 
percentage 
declined 
slightly 
from 
65% 
in 
2000 
to 
62% 
in 
2002 
for 
the 
poorest 
quintile, 
but 
increased 
from 
44% 
to 
60% 
among families 
in 
the 
second 
quintile. 
Given 
free 
access 
to 
medical 
attention 
for 
infants 
and 
young children 
through 
the 
basic 
health 
insurance program, 
poor 
families 
are less pressed 
to 
defer 
these 
services 
as 
a 
discretionary 
expense. 
Over 
one 
fourth 
of 
households 
in 
the 
bottom 
quintiles 
said 
to 
have benefited 
from 
the 
basic 
health 
insurance, 
compared 
to 
16% 
in 
the 
richest 
quintile.

9. 
In 
order 
to 
avoid potentially 
irreversible 
negative impacts 
on 
early 
development 
and 
the 
accumulation 
of 
human 
capital 
of 
poor  children 
it 
is 
necessary 
to 
support 
adequate 
mechanisms and 
instruments 
targeted 
to 
the 
poorest groups 
of 
the 
population, 
through 
specific 
measures 
that 
effectively 
safeguard resources 
for 
social 
programs 
and 
strengthen 
safety 
nets. 
This 
not 
only 
cushions 
the 
negative 
impact 
of 
increases 
in 
transitory 
poverty 
but 
can also 
prevent 
poverty 
traps 
that 
may 
hamper 
future 
economic 
growth.

Page  49
ANNEX 
4: 
BOLIVIA 
SOCIAL 
SAFETY 
NET 
STRUCTURAL 
ADJUSTMENT 
CREDIT 
SOCIAL 
SPENDING 
AND 
BUDGET 
PROTECTION 
INTRODUCTION 
1. 
The 
recent 
economic  decline resulted 
in 
strong 
negative 
impacts,  especially 
for 
the 
poor, 
given 
the 
lack 
of 
an 
effective 
public 
social 
protection 
scheme. 
As 
a result, 
social 
cohesion 
has 
weakened 
and 
civil 
conflicts 
have 
increased 
in 
Bolivia. 
While 
public 
income 
decreases 
and 
pressures 
to 
reduce 
public 
spending 
increase, 
there 
are  also 
amplified 
demands 
for 
more 
social  benefits 
for 
the 
poor 
and 
vulnerable. 
Therefore, 
additional efforts 
are 
needed 
to 
prevent 
deeper 
impacts 
from 
the 
crisis through 
a 
more 
effective and 
self-operating 
safety 
net 
that 
ensures 
not 
only 
appropriate 
targeting, 
but 
also 
improves 
the 
efficiency 
and 
effectiveness 
of 
social spending 
to 
protect 
the 
poor without 
distorting 
the 
macroeconomic  stability. 
Social Protection 
refers 
to 
a 
set 
of 
programs 
designed 
to 
help 
people 
or 
households 
to 
mitigate 
and 
cope 
with 
poverty 
or 
temporary 
income 
reductions  and 
consists 
of 
two 
components: 
(i) 
social 
assistance 
programs 
(transfers, 
subsidies 
and 
workfare 
programs)  designed 
to 
help 
people 
to 
moderate 
risks 
related 
to 
income reduction 
and 
poverty; 
and 
(ii) 
social 
safety 
net 
programs 
(old-age, 
health, 
and 
unemployment 
insurance designed 
to 
mitigate 
the 
risks 
of 
eventual 
income 
reductions). 
2. 
Based 
on 
the 
lessons 
learned 
in 
the 
implementation 
of 
public 
investment 
programs, 
effective 
budget 
protection 
programs 
must 
not only 
consider 
the 
priorities 
regarding 
social 
programs, 
but 
also 
their effective 
implementation 
throughout 
the 
entire 
management 
process, 
notably: 
(i) 
the 
evolution 
of 
the 
different 
components 
of 
the 
social 
public 
per 
capita spending 
(total, 
health, 
and 
education) 
is 
subject 
to 
greater 
fluctuation 
than 
the 
per 
capita 
GDP; 
and 
(ii) 
overall,  they show 
a 
pro-cyclical 
behavior. 
This 
Annex 
deals 
with 
the 
need 
to 
protect 
key 
items 
in 
the 
social spending 
budget 
in 
order 
to 
improve 
the 
Government’s 
ability 
to 
promote 
an 
effective 
safety 
net 
and 
implement 
anti-cyclical 
policies. 
The 
proper 
operation 
of 
this 
network 
depends 
on 
appropriate 
funding 
(external 
and law-mandated 
internal 
resources) 
and 
permanent 
institutions 
(such 
as 
Investment 
and 
Development 
Funds) 
to 
accomplish 
quick 
disbursements 
when 
needed. 
Section 
2 
discusses the 
impact 
of 
structural 
reforms 
on 
public 
investment. 
Section 
3 
analyzes 
the 
Basic Social 
Spending, 
showing 
that 
social 
spending 
is 
volatile  and 
pro-cyclical, 
while 
total 
spending 
is 
more 
constant. 
Section 
4 
shows 
budget 
protection 
in 
relation 
to 
safety 
nets 
and 
identifies 
the selection 
criteria 
for 
the 
protected  and 
eligible 
programs. 
Finally, 
Section 
5 
addresses 
the 
expected results 
from 
budget 
protection. 
STRUCTURAL REFORMS 
AND 
THEIR IMPACT ON 
PUBLIC INVESTMENT 
3. 
During 
the 
last 
few 
years, 
Bolivia 
has 
been 
implementing 
structural reforms 
to 
set 
the  basis 
for 
a  sustained 
economic 
growth 
and 
social  development 
in 
a 
situation 
of 
economic  stability. 
As 
a result, 
public 
investment 
schemes 
have 
changed 
dramatically 
39 
Page  50
since  the 
1990s, 
with 
increases 
in 
public 
social  spending 
(Le., 
investment 
in 
health 
doubled 
during 
this 
period, 
and 
investment 
in 
education  almost 
tripled 
in 
the 
same 
period) and 
decreases 
in 
productive investments. 
Overall, 
investments 
in 
infrastructure 
remained 
relatively 
stable, 
but 
with 
increases 
in 
transport 
infrastructure 
(roads) 
which 
has 
become the 
main 
public 
investment 
item 
(refer 
to 
Table 
1).

4. 
The 
2003 
budget 
forecasts 
an 
increase 
in 
public 
investment 
to 
US$637 
million 
(similar 
to 
the 
level 
executed 
in 
2001). 
The 
aim 
is 
to 
implement 
an 
anti-cyclical fiscal 
policy 
in 
order 
to 
recover 
the 
aggregate  demand, 
while 
following 
the 
criteria 
of 
prioritizing 
social 
and 
road 
building 
sectors. 
Current 
public 
investment 
schemes 
are 
consistent 
with 
the need 
to 
target 
social 
spending towards 
the 
poor 
and 
follow 
the 
Bolivia 
Plan 
(Plan 
Obrus 
con 
Empleos). 
The 
main 
programs 
of 
this 
Plan 
are: 
(i) 
road 
infrastructure, 
(ii) 
housing 
construction, 
(iii) 
residential 
gas 
connections, 
(iv)  rural 
electrification, 
(v) 
irrigation 
systems, 
(vi) 
education, 
(vii) 
health, 
(viii) 
basic sanitation, 
(ix) 
productive 
transformation 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
- 
productive municipality, 
and 
(x) 
emergency 
work 
programs 
such 
as 
anti-cyclical 
measures 
to 
create 
jobs 
and 
income 
during 
economic contractions. 
5. 
Under 
the 
decentralization 
process 
launched 
in 
1994, 
the 
Bolivian 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy 
(EBRP) 
and 
the 
National Dialogue 
Law 
(Ley 
del 
Didlogo 
Nacional 
2000) 
transferred 
responsibilities 
and 
competencies 
to 
regions 
and 
municipalities 
and 
thus, 
decentralized levels gained relevance 
as 
implementers 
and 
financial 
suppoters 
of 
the 
investments (refer 
to 
Table 
2).

BASIC 
SOCIAL 
PUBLIC 
SPENDING 
Increased Allocation 
6. 
Basic 
social 
spending4 
is 
allocated 
to 
services 
that cover 
the 
basic 
needs 
of 
the 
population, 
such 
as 
primary 
education, 
primary 
health 
care 
(immunization,  women 
and 
child 
health 
care, 
micronutrients) 
and 
basic water and sanitation  services. 
Basic social 
spending increased 
from 
US$605 
million 
in 
1995 
to 
US$970 
million 
in 
2002, 
which 
reflects 
an 
increase 
of 
3.3% 
as 
a 
share 
of 
GDP 
in 
the 
same 
period 
(refer 
to 
Table 
3). 
The 
most 
significant improvement 
took 
place 
in 
capital 
expenditures: 
which 
increased 
to 
2% 
of 
GDP 
mainly 
due 
to 
the 
increase 
in 
current  expenditures 
in 
education  and 
rural 
development. Recurrent social expenditures increased 
to 
1.3% 
of 
the 
GDP 
during 
the 
same 
period, 
featuring 
outstanding  expenditure  increments 
in 
education 
and  health. 
Social 
spending 
levels 
also 
increased 
as 
a 
share 
of 
total public 
spending, 
from 
26.7% 
in 
1995 
to 
33.3% 
in 
2002. 
For 
2003, 
total 
expenditures 
are 
estimated 
at 
US$976 
million: 
which 
is 
12.9% 
of 
GDP 
and 
32.4% 
of 
total public 
spending. 
Basic social 
spending 
is 
the 
sum 
of 
all 
public 
expenditures 
allocated 
to 
social 
sectors, 
excluding 
public 
In 
addition 
to public 
investments made 
by 
VIPFE, 
social 
capital 
expenditures 
include 
agricultural, 
4 
universities 
and meritorious 
subsidies, 
according 
to 
the 
definition 
used 
by 
the 
IMF. 
secondary and 
tertiary 
roads, 
and 
rural 
energy, 
and 
as 
from 
2002, 
funds 
from 
HIPC 
I1 
debt 
relief 
executed 
by 
municipalities. 
This 
amount 
does 
not 
consider 
wage 
bill 
increases 
in 
education and health 
included 
in 
the 
2003 
budget 
contingency 
items, 
which 
will 
increase 
recurrent 
social 
spending.

Since 
1995, 
executed 
public 
investments reached 
budgeted levels 
only 
during 
1996 
and 
2001 
(refer 
to 
Table 
4). 
The 
budgeted 
agricultural 
level 
(rural 
development) 
- 
recently 
the 
most 
important 
item 
within 
the 
productive 
sector- 
failed to 
reach 
90% 
in 
its 
execution. 
In 
infrastructure, execution 
of 
road construction 
was 
high, 
reaching 
an 
overall 
figure 
over 
90% 
of 
its 
budget. The 
execution 
of 
public 
investments 
in 
the 
social 
sector 
shows  reasonable 
levels 
and 
in 
some 
years 
it 
even  exceeded 
the 
budgeted 
levels 
in 
education, 
basic sanitation, and housing, 
while 
the 
health 
sector 
overall 
shows 
lower 
than 
budgeted 
execution 
levels. 
An 
effective 
budget 
protection 
program 
should 
not 
only 
take 
into 
account the 
prioritization 
of 
basic social programs 
in 
the 
budget 
(budgetary 
registry 
and 
certification), 
but 
also 
effective execution throughout 
the management period.

8. 
Social 
public

should

anti-cyclical 
behavior. 
This 
means 
that 
it 
should 
increase 
during 
recessions 
to 
offset  decline 
in 
incomes 
and 
decrease 
during 
economic 
expansions. 
However, 
past 
performance 
shows that 
social 
spending 
is 
cut 
in 
the 
face 
of 
lower 
fiscal 
income. 
In 
per 
capita 
terms, 
social 
spending 
increased 
from 
US$81 
per capita 
in 
1995 
to 
US$130 
per capita 
in 
2002, 
which 
is 
similar 
to 
the 
2003 
projections 
(refer 
to 
Table 
5). 
Education 
features 
the highest spending 
level, 
reaching 
almost 
US$60 
per capita 
in 
2002, 
followed 
by 
health 
with 
US$36 
per 
capita. 
After 
the 
1999 crisis, 
per 
capita 
output 
decreased 
and 
although 
the 
social 
per 
capita spending increased 
up 
until 
2001, 
this 
trend 
could 
not

in

showing 
a 
cyclical 
trend 
in 
social 
spending 
with 
above 
GDP 
fluctuations 
(refer 
to

9. 
The 
failure  to 
prioritize 
and 
plan 
social  spending results 
in 
instability 
and 
in 
reduced 
efficiency 
in 
program 
implementation. 
The 
variability 
ratio7 
used 
reveals that 
both 
between 
1995 
and 
1998 and between  1999 and 
2002, 
social 
spending 
was 
less 
variable  than 
total 
spending  (refer 
to 
Table 
6). 
Broken 
down 
into  its 
components, 
volatility 
of 
the 
different 
items 
of 
social capital 
expenditures 
exceeds 
that 
of 
recurrent 
expenditures 
-which 
consist 
mainly 
of 
salaries 
and  feature 
rigidities 
regarding 
the 
variations 
of 
fiscal 
revenues. 
Social 
sectors 
show 
sharp 
year 
to 
year 
variations, due 
to 
the 
following 
main 
reasons: 
(i) 
the 
execution 
profiles 
of 
investment projects, 
which 
have 
specific 
life 
cycles 
(for 
example, 
Education 
Sector 
Reform), 
(ii) 
management 
implementation 
capacity, 
(iii) 
unstable  basic 
bidding 
and 
hiring 
standards, 
and 
(iv) 
discretional 
political prioritization 
in 
resource 
allocation. 
The 
struggle 
for 
resources 
based 
on 
the 
above 
mentioned 
features 
is 
intensified 
due 
to fixed 
budget 
ceilings 
that 
are 
consistent 
with 
the 
macroeconomic  balance. 
These 
findings 
suggest 
that 
program 
protection must 
be 
carried 
out 
not only 
on 
an 
aggregate 
amount 
basis, 
but 
also 
on 
a per 
The 
variability ratio 
is 
estimated 
as 
the 
division 
between 
the standard 
deviation 
and 
the 
spending 
variable 
7 
average, 
i.e., 
the 
higher 
the 
percentage, the 
higher 
the 
volatility 
of 
spending. 
44 
Page  55
program 
basis, 
in 
order 
to 
prevent offsets 
that 
may 
damage 
certain 
program 
recipients 
to 
the 
benefit 
of 
others.

reduction- 
from 
becoming 
fiscal

to

irregularly

in

(PLANE, 
Plan Nacional 
de 
Empleo de Emergencia).

selected 
programs 
account 
for 
41 
% 
of

public

included 
in 
the 2003

protection involves 
a

of 
social investment. Expenditures related 
to

for

PROSIN:

Program

to

(PL 
480),

PAN:

(up

in

Nutrition

and

It

/

The 
Solidarity Bonus (BONOSOL) 
provides (non contributory) 
cash transfers 
to 
the 
elderly 
(65 years 
and 
up), 
thus 
becoming 
a 
protection 
for 
approximately 
360,000 
beneficiaries 
of 
this 
vulnerable 
segment. 
BONOSOL 
was 
established 
in 
1997 at 
annual 
Bs 
1,800 
(US$240) 
for all Bolivian 
residents 
who 
reached 
legal 
majority 
age 
(21 
years 
old) 
as 
of 
12/31/1995. 
This 
is 
a 
universal 
program,  since 
it 
covers 
the 
entire 
population,  even 
those 
covered 
by 
the 
pension 
scheme. 
In 
1998, 
this 
benefit 
was 
replaced 
by 
the 
BOLIVIDA, 
a 
program 
consisting 
of 
a 
benefit 
of 
approximately 
US$60 
that 
remained 
in 
force 
until 
2002, 
when 
the 
initial 
BONOSOL 
was 
reinstated 
for 
an 
amount 
of 
Bs 
1800 that 
will 
be 
kept unmodified 
until 
2008. 
In 
1997, 
funds 
allocated 
to 
BONOSOL 
amounted 
to 
US$90 
million, being 
reduced 
with 
the 
BOLIVIDA 
48 
Page  59
program to 
an 
annual 
average 
below US$30 
million 
between 1998 
and 
2002. 
For 
year 
2003, 
estimates 
amount 
to US$90 
million 
and sustain 
an 
anti-cyclical 
policy. 
Funding 
is 
extra-budgetary, since 
resources 
come 
from 
the

on

of

Epidemiological 
Shield: 
The 
purpose 
of 
this 
program 
is 
to

and

surveillance 
system. Diseases 
are: 
malaria,

others. 
In

34%

Mother-Child

and

10% 
of

resources 
from

Special

2000 
account 
for

same

when 
joint 
tax 
resources 
run 
short 
(Municipal

Immunization  Program

with 
no 
external 
funding. 
PLANE 
IZ 
is 
a temporary emergency employment 
strategy. 
Major 
components 
are 
the 
Service 
Employment 
Program 
(PES, 
Programa 
de 
Empleo 
en 
Servicios) 
for 
urban 
areas, 
and 
the 
Rural 
Employment Program 
(PER, 
Programa 
de 
Empleo 
Rural), 
which 
later 
on 
has 
become  the 
Works 
with 
Employment  Program 
(POE, 
Programa 
de 
Obras 
con 
Empleo). 
The 
PLANE 
I1 
Program provides 
funding 
for 
unskilled 
labor 
for 
projects or 
activities 
of 
public 
use. 
The 
second phase 
of 
the 
program 
started 
in 
2003, 
promoting 
better 
targeting 
and 
introducing 
transparency 
criteria 
in 
the 
selection 
of 
projects 
and 
beneficiaries. The 
minimum 
protected budget 
amounts 
to 
US$25 
million, 
a 
similar 
level 
to 
what 
was 
executed 
during 
the 
previous 
year. 
A 
performance 
of 
approximately 
100% 
is 
expected and 
funding 
is 
provided 
mainly 
through grants 
and 
external 
credit. 
EXPECTED 
OUTCOMES 
20. 
Program protection 
is 
expected 
to 
have 
the 
following 
outcomes: 
i) 
mitigate 
negative impacts 
resulting 
from 
the crisis; 
ii) 
continue 
increasing 
enrollment 
rates; 
iii) 
continue

and

rates;

the

of

and

and

mitigate 
unemployment 
and 
poverty.

BACKGROUND 
1. 
The 
economic 
decline, 
which 
began 
by 
the 
end 
of 
1998 
in 
Bolivia, 
had 
adverse 
impacts 
on 
employment. 
Open 
unemployment 
rates 
overall 
increased 
from 
4.33% 
in 
1999 
to 
5.24% 
in 
2001, 
while 
in 
the 
country’s 
major 
urban 
centers 
during 
the 
same 
period, 
these 
rates 
increased 
from 
6.1 
% 
to 
9.4% 
for 
the 
entire 
population 
and 
from 
6.9% 
to 
10.6% 
for 
women. 
2. 
The 
main 
factors 
that 
explain 
poverty 
increases 
in 
Bolivia 
between 1999 
and 
2001 
have 
been 
income 
drops 
in 
urban 
areas 
and 
increasing 
costs 
in 
rural 
areas. 
In 
constant 
terms, 
incomes 
fell 
by 
3.1% 
in 
urban 
areas 
and 
costs 
increased 
by 
0.6% 
in 
rural 
areas 
(refer 
to 
Bolivia 
poverty 
note, 
2002). 
3. 
These 
high 
unemployment 
rates 
were 
coupled 
by 
an  increase 
in 
informal 
employment. 
During 
2000, 
the 
informal 
sector 
accounted 
for 
69% 
of 
all 
urban 
and 
rural 
employment, 
including 
small agricultural 
and 
livestock 
producers 
on 
own 
lands. 
This 
percentage 
has 
increased 
relative 
to 
1997, 
with 
people 
formally 
employed 
(employees 
or 
employers) 
turning 
to 
the 
informal 
sector 
or 
to open unemployment. 
4. 
This 
employment  crisis 
and 
increasing 
unemployment 
rates 
have 
resulted 
in 
a 
drop 
in 
real 
income 
for 
the population, 
with 
severe 
consequences 
especially 
for 
poorer 
urban 
residents, 
who 
have 
minimum 
non-employment income 
options 
or 
none 
at 
all. 
5. 
The social 
crisis 
in 
Bolivia 
has 
led 
to conflicts 
that demonstrate 
the 
dissatisfaction 
of 
the 
population, 
who 
demands 
salary 
increases, 
higher 
investments 
in 
economic 
and 
social infrastructure, and 
opportunities 
for 
the 
poor. 
Mitigation 
of 
the 
Impact 
of 
Unemployment 
6. 
Given 
the 
current  economic  setting 
in 
Bolivia 
and 
considering 
that 
the 
efforts 
made 
by 
the 
Government 
to 
address 
this 
crisis 
will 
not 
have 
an 
impact 
perceivable 
as 
such 
by 
the 
population 
in 
the 
immediate 
or 
short-term, 
the 
emergency 
workfare 
strategy 
(PLANE) 
must 
continue. 
This 
strategy 
was 
implemented 
during 
2002  and 
had 
some 
satisfactory 
outcomes 
in 
the 
effectiveness 
and 
efficiency 
of 
its 
main 
objective; 
the 
massive 
and 
timely 
coverage 
of 
poorer 
groups. 
7. 
This 
National 
Plan  for 
Emergency 
Employment 
(PLANE, 
Plan 
Nacional 
de 
Empleo de 
Emergencia) 
is 
an 
anti-cyclical 
mechanism 
that 
can be 
expanded 
or 
contracted 
in 
accord 
with 
economic developments. The 
flexibility 
of 
this 
workfare program 
is 
based 
on 
the  amount 
of 
PLANE’S 
monthly 
payment 
to 
the 
beneficiaries, 
such 
that 
if 
the 
economy 
recovers, 
they 
can 
shift 
to 
better options 
offered 
by 
the 
formal 
labor 
market 
or 
55 
Page  66
to 
other better 
paying 
alternatives. 
Consequently, 
the 
monthly 
pay 
offered 
to 
PLANE 
workers 
is 
the 
main 
self-targeting  variable 
and 
its 
major 
feature 
is 
an 
anti-cyclical 
program.

10. 
Bolivia 
Plan: 
In 
response 
to 
the 
national 
emergency, 
the 
political 
parties 
of 
the 
Government have 
agreed 
on 
the 
development 
of 
the 
so-called “Government 
of 
National 
Responsibility” 
based 
on 
a practical 
synthesis 
of 
the 
“Emergency 
Plan” 
and 
the 
“Working 
Plan 
- 
Bolivia 
for 
Bolivians.” 
This 
is 
called 
the 
BOLIVIA 
PLAN 
and 
its 
main 
purpose 
is 
to 
generate 
employment 
and 
promote 
growth 
and  development 
with 
social 
equity, 
acknowledging 
the 
right 
to 
participation 
and 
decision-making 
of 
all 
Bolivians 
citizens. 
The 
main 
aspects 
of 
this 
PLAN 
are 
as 
follows: 
Address 
unemployment 
-the 
major 
problem 
identified 
by 
Bolivian 
citizens- 
both 
in 
urban 
and 
rural 
areas, 
by 
means 
of 
three 
strategies: 
(i) 
investment 
policy 
for 
the 
construction 
of 
infrastructure 
works 
and 
basic 
services, 
residential 
gas, 
irrigation 
systems 
and 
rural 
electrification 
(workfare 
programs); 
(ii) 
promotion 
of 
private activities 
and 
investments 
through 
public 
and 
private 
funding 
mechanisms 
for productive 
and 
technological 
adjustment, 
market 
search, 
and accessible 
productive 
credits; and 
(iii) 
special 
emphasis 
on 
the 
development 
of 
tourism, 
eco-tourism 
and 
environment-related activities. 
Define 
a 
Governmental 
policy 
regarding 
gas 
and  hydrocarbons, 
their 
industrialization 
and massive 
consumption, 
to 
the 
benefit 
of 
the 
Bolivians. 
Revisit, 
improve 
and 
modify 
the 
capitalization 
process 
to 
the 
benefit 
of 
the 
nation.  Payment 
of 
“Bonosol” 
and 
studying 
the 
future 
channeling 
of

Collective 
Capitalization 
Fund 
as 
a

for

in

and

Additionally, 
reestablish 
railway 
services, 
with 
a 
rational 
focus 
on 
addressing 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
population. 
Develop 
a 
productive agricultural 
transformation 
policy 
to improve 
the 
living 
conditions 
of 
rural 
inhabitants 
and 
to 
strengthen 
agricultural

providing

with

and

Implement 
a Governmental

to 
improve

Futuro 
scholarship, education credits 
and

that

universal

for

Bolivia, 
with 
special 
emphasis 
on 
the

of

services 
into 
the education 
process. 
Develop 
policies 
oriented 
towards 
implementing 
Universal 
Healthcare 
Insurance. 
Implement  a 
massive social 
housing construction 
program 
with 
subsidized 
credits 
and 
long-term 
funding. 
Decisively 
fight 
corruption 
through 
actions  that  strengthen the  Judiciary, 
enforce 
the 
law, 
and 
put 
an 
end 
to 
impunity. 
Immediately 
and deeply 
reform 
the 
Political 
Constitution 
in 
response 
to 
the 
legitimate 
demands 
and 
aspirations 
of 
all  Bolivian 
citizens, 
with 
the

Page  68
introduction 
of

Constitutional 
Referendum 
and 
other

that 
secure

Develop

for

and

of

of

given

original

in

the

Secure

of

and

Acknowledge

power

for

11. 
Most 
of 
the 
options 
to 
overcome 
the 
Bolivian 
economic and social 
crisis 
require 
huge 
amounts 
of 
resources. 
Under 
Bolivia’s 
current 
scenario, these 
resources 
will 
not 
be 
possible 
to 
obtain 
in 
the 
short 
term. Therefore,  other options  have been considered 
- 
which 
can 
be 
implemented 
with 
available 
resources- 
to 
mitigate 
the 
impact 
of 
income 
drops 
on 
the 
poorest 
segments. 
The 
mechanisms that have been considered 
are 
to: 
(i) 
continue 
with 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
national 
plan for 
emergency employment, 
plane, 
(ii) 
implement 
adjustments 
in 
the 
operational 
processes 
of 
municipal 
projects that have 
already been allocated 
resources, 
so 
as 
to 
hasten 
their 
implementation, 
or 
to 
modify 
the 
technical 
conditions 
to 
make 
them more 
labor-intensive;  and 
to 
(iii) 
make adjustments 
to 
the 
co-funding 
conditions 
of 
municipal 
investments. 
Resources 
to 
fund 
infrastructure 
projects 
intensive 
in 
unskilled 
labor 
will 
be 
sought 
in 
the 
medium-term.

Bolivia 
safety 
net. 
These 
immediate 
and

as 
components 
of

PROGRAMS 
TO 
BE

NATIONAL PLAN 
FOR

EMPLOYMENT 
- 
PLANE 
General 
13. 
PLANE 
is 
an 
anti-cyclical 
strategy 
to 
reduce 
the 
impact 
of 
income 
drops 
from 
employment 
losses 
on 
the 
country’s  most vulnerable population. 
Hence, 
PLANE 
funds 
the 
use 
of 
unskilled 
labor 
in 
the 
construction and 
development 
of 
community 
projects 
and 
activities, 
both 
in 
rural 
and 
urban 
areas. 
These 
projects 
arise 
from 
the 
need 
to 
improve 
the 
conditions 
of 
public 
infrastructure, 
especially 
those 
that 
have 
an 
impact 
on 
poorer 
communities. 
Some 
of 
the 
conditions 
and features that 
allow 
for 
the 
compliance 
of 
this 
purpose 
are 
the 
following:

Page  69
(a) 
PLANE 
offers 
a 
monthly 
pav 
of 
B$480 
per 
worker. 
This 
amount 
ensures 
self- 
targeting, 
so 
that 
beneficiaries 
are 
effectively 
the 
poorest 
segment 
of 
the 
population. 
This 
is 
not 
only 
related 
to 
the 
minimum 
wage  established 
in 
the 
country, 
but 
also 
to 
the 
distribution 
of 
wages 
for 
working 
age 
population, 
by 
income level. 
The 
Bolivian 
Government 
considers 
that 
this 
payment variable 
has 
been 
correctly 
estimated 
as 
a 
control 
of 
PLANE 
targeting  towards 
the  poorest 
population, 
since 
it 
is 
assumed 
that 
those 
who 
access 
the 
program 
have 
no 
immediate 
or 
better 
income 
alternatives.  Furthermore, 
this 
feature  makes  the 
program 
anti-cyclical, 
to 
the 
extent 
that 
if 
the 
economy 
recovers, 
better 
income 
opportunities 
will 
appear 
and 
PLANE 
beneficiaries 
will 
be 
able 
to 
access 
them. 
(b) 
PLANE 
benefits 
from 
unskilled 
labor 
for 
small communitv 
projects. 
Given 
their 
features, 
the 
projects 
funded 
by 
PLANE 
have 
very 
high 
efficiency 
levels 
regarding their 
implementation. 
These 
projects 
are 
prepared 
with 
simple 
profiles, 
assessed 
and 
approved 
under 
minimum 
compliance criteria, 
and 
easy 
in 
terms 
of 
beneficiary 
allocation, 
among 
other 
features. 
In

the 
different 
projects 
are 
selected, 
not 
only 
based 
on 
their simplicity, 
but 
also 
under 
community 
well-being 
criteria, 
so 
as 
to 
benefit 
the 
entire 
community 
involved. 
(c) 
PLANE 
is 
implemented under 
an 
external  operating  and 
institutional 
scheme. 
PLANE 
is 
controlled 
by 
a 
Funding 
Board 
(Directorio 
hico 
de 
Fondos 
-DUF), 
but 
its 
direct 
management 
and

are 
outsourced 
to 
private 
institutions. 
This 
has 
facilitated 
its

especially 
due 
to 
the 
considerable 
flexibility 
of 
the 
operation 
standards 
that regulate 
these 
institutions 
compared 
to 
those 
of 
public 
agencies. 
PLANE 
management 
is 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
UNDP 
and 
the 
operation 
and 
intermediation 
is 
undertaken 
by 
three 
microcredit 
organizations 
called 
“wholesalers”, 
who 
have 
a 
direct relationship 
with 
program 
beneficiaries. 
(d) 
Implementation 
procedures 
have  been 
designed 
under  celerity-related  criteria. 
Project 
profiles 
are 
prepared 
by

or

according 
to 
their 
needs 
and

and 
based

program

These

are 
submitted 
to 
the

of 
the 
wholesalers, 
who 
send

to 
management

their

beneficiaries 
are 
allocated 
to 
each 
project 
selected 
from 
a 
beneficiary 
database 
held 
by

Works 
must 
begin 
3 
days 
after  the 
notice 
to 
the  proponent 
(municipality 
or 
prefecture) 
has 
been 
delivered. Payments 
are 
made 
every 
two 
weeks based 
on 
the 
report 
issued 
by 
the 
supervisors. 
To 
authorize 
payment,  supervisors 
monitor 
beneficiary 
attendance 
and project 
progresses. 
The municipality 
is 
the 
implementing 
agency responsible 
for 
the 
project.

NATIONAL 
SOCIAL AND

-

29. 
The social 
emergency 
fund, 
FSE, 
began 
its 
operations 
in 
1987 
as 
a 
compensation 
mechanism 
for 
structural 
reform 
measures. 
Its 
main 
purposes 
were 
to 
reduce 
unemployment 
and 
increase 
household income. Starting 
in 
1991, 
this 
fund 
became 
the 
social investment 
fund 
-FIS-  that 
operates 
under 
three 
stages. 
The 
objectives and focus 
of 
the 
FIS 
are 
adjusted based 
on 
the 
priorities 
of 
social investment, 
such 
as: 
greater coverage 
for 
poverty  alleviation 
and 
the 
improvement 
of 
living 
conditions 
based 
on 
human 
development. 
30. 
The 
National  Dialogue  Law, 
issued 
in 
July 
of 
2001, 
was 
passed 
under 
the 
Bolivian 
Strategy 
for 
Poverty  Reduction 
(Estrategia 
Boliviana 
de  Reduccidn 
de 
la 
Pobreza 
- 
EBRP), 
as 
a 
basic 
guideline 
for 
EBRP 
management. 
This 
law 
improves 
the 
competencies  and 
capacities 
of 
each 
governmental 
organization 
to 
comply 
with 
the 
principles 
and  goals 
of 
the 
EBRP. 
Additionally, 
criteria 
applicable 
to 
resource 
reallocation 
among 
priority 
sectors 
have  been 
set; 
greater 
responsibilities  regarding 
resource 
use 
are 
devolved 
to 
the 
Municipal 
Governments; and 
a 
mandate 
is 
established 
for 
the 
investment 
in 
social 
and productive 
municipal 
programs  and projects,  thereby 
closing 
the 
gap 
proposed 
in 
the 
same 
EBRP 
between 
the 
economic 
and 
social 
agendas. 
3 
1. 
The 
Dialogue 
Law 
further

“National

Nacional

that  “supports 
the

are 
provided 
by

to

Municipalities, 
thereby 
promoting 
accountability 
and 
equity 
in 
transfers  allocated 
for 
public 
investment 
purposes 
...” 
32. 
This 
policy 
(PNC) 
modified 
the

of 
the 
National 
Fund 
for

Development 
(Fondo

Fund 
(Fondo Nacional 
de

Productiva 
y 
Social 
-

as 
“a

development

public 
law 
agency, 
structured 
as 
a 
former 
Social Investment 
Fund, 
under 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
President’s 
Office, 
with 
national 
competencies and deconcentrated operations. 
33. 
Basic 
FPS

are 
assigned 
under 
the

provided,

to

The

developed

Law

reference. 
Resources

of

population, considering 
efliciency, 
transparency 
and 
equity 
criteria, 
and 
promoting

Institutional 
Development 
Programs 
for

34.

policy 
(consejo

de

econdmica 
y 
social 
- 
conapes) 
modified 
the 
local 
counterpart 
fund 
rates 
for 
the  support 
of 
municipal 
level 
projects. 
The new 
counterpart 
rates 
are 
of 
20%,  15% 
and 
lo%, 
based 
on 
the 
sector 
and 
the 
5 
poverty 
ranks 
of

municipalities. 
Former

ranged 
between 
50% 
and 
lo%,

in

1,2 
and 
3.

I1

-

General 
sense 
of 
transparency 
5 
1 
% 
of

50%

than

37%

59%

80%

In

Background 
for 
FPS 
Conditions 
43. 
Bearing

of

should

for

At

FPS 
should

an

For

shall

experience 
implemented

I. 
This  program 
consists 
of 
work 
implementation 
by 
the 
community 
with 
the 
support 
of 
a 
local NGO. 
This 
method had 
the

(i) 
reducing  works’ 
costs, 
(ii) 
favoring 
the 
participation 
of 
beneficiaries 
in 
the 
service, and 
(iii) 
promoting 
the 
use 
of 
local 
labor. 
45. 
The

of

these 
options 
will 
be

on

and  features 
of

works 
(maximum

and

specifications 
of 
the 
projects), 
as 
per

of 
project 
and 
applicable 
requirements 
and 
conditions. 
The 
proposal

Page  75
shall

looking for 
potential

sources 
and

will

Assuring the budget 
for 
priority 
programs 
4

generated 
an

In

is 
necessary 
to 
guarantee

in

In

Bolivia

5%

access. 
This 
program 
is 
key 
for 
the 
country 
to 
continue 
to 
increase coverage 
and 
reduce 
mother 
and 
child 
mortality, 
thereby 
effectively 
dealing 
with 
health 
problems that 
cause 
grave economic effects 
on 
families, especially 
during 
a crisis. 
12. 
Extensa  consists 
of 
the 
conformation 
of 
mobile 
health 
brigades 
(Brisas), 
composed 
of 
doctors, 
nurses 
and 
dentists 
who 
are 
based 
in 
health 
centers 
or 
local 
hospitals 
and 
attend 
the 
surrounding  communities. 
These 
brigades 
will 
connect 
with 
Asistes, 
local 
health promoters 
who 
carry out 
basic 
health 
promotion 
and organization 
activities 
in 
each 
community.  The program 
would 
also 
consider 
teams 
to 
reinforce 
the 
capacities 
of 
local 
hospital 
and 
health 
centers 
in 
the 
intervention 
areas. 
13. 
Extensa began 
its 
activities 
with 
the 
present 
government  and 
is 
currently 
in 
a 
phase 
of 
implementation 
and 
expansion 
of 
its 
coverage. 
Guaranteeing 
its 
budget 
will 
allow 
that 
the 
brigades 
and Asistes, 
(currently 
almost 
all 
of 
them 
are 
already 
hired) 
are 
maintained 
and 
equipped appropriately, 
as 
well 
as 
the 
health 
facilities 
in 
which 
they 
are 
based. 
14. 
Given 
that 
the 
program 
is 
geared 
towards 
providing 
health 
services 
to 
rural 
and 
indigenous communities, 
it 
is 
vital 
that 
the 
program 
develops 
in 
such 
a 
way 
that 
it 
is 
flexible 
to 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
community. 
In 
this 
sense, 
the 
program 
has 
been selecting 
the 
Brisas 
and 
Asistes 
with 
participation 
of 
local 
communities 
and 
indigenous 
people 
organizations. 
In 
the 
framework 
of 
this 
program, 
this 
participation 
is 
going  to 
be 
formalized 
through 
the approval 
of 
a 
Manual 
stating that 
the 
Asistes 
will 
be 
chosen 
from 
the postulants presented 
by 
their 
communities 
and 
selected 
with 
its 
participation. 
The 
communities 
will 
also 
participate 
in 
an 
annual 
evaluation 
of 
their 
performance 
that 
will 
support 
the 
recognition 
of 
non 
pecuniary 
incentives. 
68 
Page  79
Management 
Agreements 
and 
Decentralization 
15. 
The 
Bolivian 
government 
began 
a 
significant 
and 
innovative 
decentralization 
process 
in 
1994. 
The 
decentralization, however, 
created 
certain 
challenges 
with 
respect 
to 
accountability 
and 
definition 
of 
responsibilities 
for 
health, 
between 
the 
different 
government levels. 
In 
this 
context, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Health 
introduced 
the 
Management 
Agreements 
(or 
Performance 
Agreements) 
as 
a 
measure 
to 
keep 
and 
improve 
its 
relationship 
with 
the 
other 
levels 
of 
government. 
Thus, 
according 
to 
the 
commitments 
that 
the 
government 
assumed 
with 
the 
international 
cooperation 
related 
to national 
health 
goals, 
the 
Health 
Ministry 
has 
been 
signing 
Management  Agreements 
with 
the 
prefectures and Departmental 
Health 
Services 
(SEDES) 
since 
1999. 
This 
policy 
has 
helped 
to 
more clearly 
focus 
the 
government 
priorities 
in 
terms 
of 
health 
outputs 
and 
outcomes, 
align 
the departmental 
governments 
with 
these 
priorities, 
give 
transparency 
to 
the 
government plans 
and 
facilitate accountability. 
16. 
Although 
these 
Management 
Agreements, 
signed between 1999 and 
2002, 
have 
been 
the 
beginning 
of 
an 
important 
reform, 
they 
have 
also 
suffered 
of 
some 
deficiencies. 
The 
principal 
changes 
that 
must 
be 
established 
in 
the 
Management Agreements 
are: 
i) 
simplifying 
and 
reducing 
the 
number 
of 
goals 
considered 
in 
them, 
particularly 
the 
process 
indicators, 
making 
the 
priorities 
and 
accountability  more 
clear; 
ii) 
establishing  clear 
criteria 
to 
set 
the 
goals; 
and 
iii) 
implementing 
a 
system 
of 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
of 
the 
Agreements. 
The 
government 
has 
manifested 
its 
will 
to 
sign 
the 
Management 
Agreements 
and 
to 
carry 
out 
the 
mentioned 
changes. 
17. 
Additionally, 
the present 
government 
has 
made 
some 
modifications 
that 
could 
potentially 
make 
the 
decentralization 
process 
more  effective. 
The 
new 
management 
model 
establishes the 
Local 
Health Directory 
- 
DILOS 
as 
the 
local 
entity 
in 
charge 
of 
setting 
the 
political 
direction 
and 
strategic 
planning 
of 
health 
service networks, 
while 
a 
network 
manager 
will 
be 
in 
charge 
of 
the 
permanent 
operational 
management 
of 
the 
health service 
network. 
The 
DILOS 
consists 
of 
a representative 
from 
the SEDES, another 
one 
of 
the 
municipality 
and one 
of 
grassroots 
organizations (Comite's 
de 
Vigilancia). 
Its 
role 
is 
to 
approve 
the 
annual operative 
plans 
and 
the 
SUM1 
payments and 
appoint 
the 
health  service 
network 
manager. 
To 
reflect  this  new 
management 
arrangements,  the 
Management  Agreements 
will 
include,  among 
the 
principal 
process 
indicators,  the 
conformation 
of 
the 
health 
service 
networks 
with 
the appointment 
of 
their 
managers and 
the 
effective 
operation 
and 
functioning 
of 
the 
DILOS.

due 
to 
the 
crisis. 
l2

“traditional 
doctors”, 
this 
document 
understands: 
native indigenous 
healers.

Page  83
ANNEX 
7: 
BOLIVIA 
SOCIAL 
SAFETY 
NET 
STRUCTURAL 
ADJUSTMENT 
CREDIT 
PROTECTING 
EDUCATION 
PROGRAMS 
AND 
REFORMS 
BACKGROUND 
1. 
In 
the 
early 
1990s, 
about 
20 
percent 
of 
the 
population 
was 
illiterate 
and 
55 
percent 
was 
functionally 
illiterate. 
Most 
lived 
in 
rural 
areas 
where 
female 
illiteracy 
was 
nearly 
50 
percent. 
The 
gross 
coverage 
of 
public primary 
education 
ranged 
from 
60 
percent 
in 
rural 
areas 
to 
87 
percent 
in 
urban 
areas. 
On 
average, 
the 
adult 
population had 
4 
years 
of 
schooling, 
only 
37 
percent 
finished 
primary 
school, 
and 
9 
percent 
of 
the 
population 
in 
rural 
areas 
attended  one 
year 
of 
school 
beyond 
the 
primary 
level. 
While  not 
many 
children 
entered 
school, 
even 
fewer 
continued. 
Of 
children 
enrolled 
in 
primary 
school, 
only 
43 
percent 
of 
urban children 
and 
11 
percent 
of 
rural 
children 
continued 
beyond 
the 
fifth 
grade. These 
indicators 
revealed 
a 
system 
plagued 
by 
gross 
inefficiencies, 
difficult 
access, 
and 
poor 
quality. 
In 
addition, 
the 
share 
of 
government expenditure 
on 
education 
was 
low 
and generally 
skewed 
toward 
higher levels 
of 
the 
system. 
In 
1991, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
started 
considering 
and 
discussing 
reform 
in 
the sector. 
The 
reform 
effort 
began 
in 
1994 
and 
has 
since 
been 
supported 
by 
successive 
administrations 
as 
state 
policy. 
EDUCATION 
REFORMS 
OF 
THE 
PREVIOUS 
GOVERNMENT 
2. 
The 
Educational 
Reform 
Program 
(PRE) 
was 
established 
by 
the 
National 
Law 
No. 
1565 
of 
July 
7, 
1994. 
The 
conceptual 
framework 
and 
actions 
of 
the 
Program coincide 
somewhat 
with 
the proposals 
formulated 
by 
various 
social organizations 
that 
participated 
in 
the 
National 
Congress 
on 
Education 
which 
preceded 
the enactment 
of 
the 
law. 
3. 
During 
the  decade 
of 
its 
implementation, 
PRE 
has 
profoundly 
transformed 
the 
education 
system 
in 
the 
following 
areas: 
(i) 
curricula 
for 
primary 
education, 
which 
have 
undergone  three 
cycles 
of 
development, 
to 
respond 
to 
basic 
learning 
needs; 
(ii) 
restructuring 
of 
the 
national 
educational 
system; 
(iii) 
strengthening 
of 
central 
administration 
and decentralization 
of 
management 
to 
municipalities; 
and 
(iv) 
community 
participation 
in 
the 
educational 
process. 
4. 
Initially 
in 
the 
mid-1990s, 
the 
reform 
focused 
on 
the 
transformation 
and 
strengthening 
of 
the 
educational 
system, 
improvement 
of 
the 
pedagogical 
quality,  and 
development 
of 
human 
resources 
in 
education. 
The 
next 
steps 
in 
education 
reform 
aimed 
at: 
(i) 
enhancing 
community 
participation 
in 
the 
educational 
process 
through 
the 
participation 
of 
parents, 
teachers, 
nucleus 
and 
district 
principals 
in 
education 
development 
programming 
at 
the 
level 
of 
municipalities  (PROME) 
and 
in 
education 
projects 
implemented 
at  the 
level 
of 
nuclear 
schools 
(PEN); 
and 
(ii) 
strengthening 
management capacities 
at 
the 
local 
level. 
The 
latter 
institutional 
development 
of 
district 
managers 
at 
the 
municipal level 
as 
the 
local 
operators 
of 
the 
education 
sector has 
helped 
to 
increase 
access 
to 
schooling and 
to 
decrease 
the 
attrition 
rates 
of 
female 
students 
in 
73 
Page  84
rural 
areas. 
Toward 
the 
late 
1990s, 
preliminary 
analysis 
on 
the 
situation 
of 
secondary 
and 
higher 
education 
was 
undertaken. 
5. 
Achievements 
of 
the 
reform 
program. 
By 
2001, 
after 
eight 
years 
of 
implementation, 
the 
illiteracy 
rate 
had 
decreased 
to 
13 
percent. 
In 
rural 
areas, 
only 
26 
percent 
of 
the 
population 
were 
illiterate 
(nearly 
11 
percentage 
points 
lower); 
in 
urban 
areas 
only 
7 
percent 
were 
illiterate. 
The 
youth literacy 
rate stood 
at 
94 
percent, 
or 
three 
percentage 
points 
above 
the 
average 
of 
the 
Latin 
America 
and 
the 
Caribbean region. 
(However, 
overall 
illiteracy 
remained 
higher than 
the 
average 
of 
the region.) 
In 
terms 
of 
enrollment, 
the 
gross 
coverage 
of 
primary 
education 
rose 
to 
108 
percent, 
and 
the 
net 
coverage 
rose 
to 
97 percent 
for both 
girls 
and 
boys, suggesting that 
Bolivia 
was 
very 
close 
to 
providing 
equal educational 
opportunity. 
Further, 
the 
completion 
rate 
and the 
graduation  rate 
increased 
while 
dropout 
rates 
decreased. 
The 
above  achievements 
reflected 
the 
effects 
of 
increases 
in 
public 
resources 
allocated 
to 
the 
sector 
over 
the 
period 
1990-99. 
The 
allocation 
increased 
to 
US$192 
million 
in 
1999, 
from 
US$78 
million 
in 
1990. 
It 
also 
reflected 
another 
effect 
of 
the 
reform, 
the 
growing 
interest 
of 
parents 
in 
the 
education 
of 
their 
children. 
6. 
Curriculum 
transformation 
is 
under 
implementation 
in 
primary 
education. 
Currently, 
instruction 
in 
the 
first 
and 
second 
cycles 
(grades 
1-5) 
follows 
the 
new 
core 
curriculum, 
while 
curricular transformation 
is 
beginning 
in 
the 
third 
cycle 
(grades 
6-8). 
The 
internal efficiency 
of 
the 
public 
school system 
has 
also 
improved 
considerably. 
From 
1997 
to 
2000, 
the 
average 
number 
of 
years 
required 
to 
complete 
grade 
5 
has 
been reduced 
from 
11 
years 
to 
7 
years, 
while 
the 
proportion 
of 
students 
completing 
grade 
8 
in 
rural 
areas 
has 
increased 
from 
8 
percent 
to 
29 
percent. 
In 
urban 
areas, 
the increase 
in 
grade 
8 
completion 
has 
been 
from 
29 
percent 
to 
64 
percent. 
In 
school management improvement, 
the 
implementation 
of 
1,194 
Educational  Improvement 
Projects 
has 
involved 
all 
3 
14 
municipal 
governments, 
which 
supported 
participatory planning 
and 
the 
municipal level 
and 
mobilized 
and 
organized 
district 
and 
school 
principals, 
teachers, 
parents and 
students. 
There  have 
also  been 
achievements 
in 
specific 
areas 
such 
as 
the 
introduction 
and 
expansion 
of 
bilingual 
education, 
design 
and 
implementation 
of 
an 
education 
information 
system, 
identification 
of 
reform 
areas 
in 
higher 
education, 
and 
promotion 
of 
education 
policy 
discussions 
with 
civil 
society. 
EDUCATION 
REFORMS 
OF 
THE 
PRESENT 
GOVERNMENT 
7. 
Strategic Sectoral 
Plan. 
To 
reach the 
international 
goals 
of 
the 
Fast 
Track-EFA 
initiative,  which 
seeks 
to 
reach 
a 
90 
percent 
completion 
rate 
in 
grade 
8 
by 
2015, 
the 
government 
has 
been 
formulating 
a 
long-term 
sector 
plan 
(2003-2010) 
which 
incorporates 
the 
goals 
of 
the 
Education 
for 
All 
@FA) initiative 
and the 
needs 
of 
the 
productive 
sector and 
the 
labor market 
within 
socio-cultural context 
of 
the 
country. 
To 
reach 
consensus 
on 
the 
plan, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
will 
undertake consultations 
with 
stakeholders, 
who 
are 
an 
important 
part 
of 
the 
educational 
process 
and 
can 
make 
more 
viable 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
plan 
proposals. 
Financing 
for 
the  programs 
to 
be 
74 
Page  85
undertaken under 
the 
strategic sectoral 
plan 
will 
be sought 
from 
national 
and 
international 
sources. 
8. 
EFA 
Fast 
Track 
Initiative. 
Within 
the 
framework 
of 
the strategic sectoral 
plan 
and the 
financing 
proposal 
to 
access 
resources 
from 
the 
EFA 
Fast 
Track Initiative, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
includes 
Beca 
Futuro, 
as 
an 
alternative 
incentive 
for 
families to 
increase 
the 
attendance 
level 
and permanence 
for 
their 
children 
at 
the 
primary 
level, 
which 
is 
also 
consistent 
with 
the 
national 
and 
international 
goals. 
To 
date, 
this envisioned 
conditional 
cash 
transfer 
program 
for 
the 
provision 
of 
scholarships targeted 
to 
the 
very 
poor 
is 
in 
active 
debate 
in 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education, 
VIPFE, 
and 
UDAPE, 
and 
consensus 
on 
the 
design 
of 
targeting 
mechanisms, 
financial 
sustainability, 
ownership 
at 
the 
municipal 
level,  and 
counter-cyclical 
character 
will 
be  achieved 
through  further 
consultation 
and 
discussion over 
the 
coming 
months. 
(Owing 
to 
this 
time 
constraint, 
the 
program 
was 
not 
incorporated 
in 
the 
matrix 
as 
a specific 
activity 
of 
the credit.) The 
Bank 
will 
be 
actively 
engaged 
in 
the 
conceptualization 
process, 
through 
the 
provision 
of 
policy 
advice  and technical 
assistance 
to 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
which 
is 
preparing 
the 
Government’s 
proposal 
for 
the 
EFA 
Fast 
Track 
initiative 
in 
which 
this 
scholarship 
program 
will 
be 
a 
significant 
component. 
9. 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy. 
Consistent 
with 
the 
Strategic 
Sector  Plan,  the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
is 
preparing 
a proposal 
to 
adjust 
the 
Bolivian 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
(BPRS), 
to 
harmonize  the 
goals 
and 
targets 
outlined 
in 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Summit 
- 
the 
Millennium 
Development 
Goals 
(MDGs) 
- 
into 
the 
updated 
BPRS. 
With 
the 
possibility 
of 
participating 
the 
Education 
For 
All 
Fast 
Track Initiative, 
the sector 
is 
well 
advanced 
in 
terms 
of 
prioritizing 
the 
actions 
needed 
to 
attain universal 
primary 
completion. 
10. 
Of 
grave concern 
is 
emerging 
evidence that 
to 
cope 
with 
the 
pressures 
brought 
about 
by 
the 
crisis, 
the 
poor 
are 
withdrawing 
children 
from 
school 
(Annex 
3). 
Government 
data 
for 
the 
three 
years 
of 
2000, 
2001,  and 
2002 
show that 
enrollment 
of 
children 
from 
the 
poorest 
income 
quintile 
has 
declined 
from 
84 
percent 
to 
80 
percent; 
regular 
attendance 
in 
school 
by 
children 
from 
the 
poorest 
families 
has 
also 
shown  a 
similar 
decline. Unless 
addressed 
in 
time 
by 
adequately targeted 
measures, 
the 
decline 
may lead 
to 
irreversible decline 
in 
the 
human 
capital 
of 
the 
poor. 
EDUCATION 
REFORM 
ACTIVITIES 
IN 
THE 
SAC 
11. 
Budget 
for 
education 
reform 
program. 
The 
main 
objective 
of 
this 
reform 
is 
to 
improve 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
school  education  and 
to 
increase the  coverage 
of 
public 
education, 
which 
is 
developed 
through 
the 
upgrading 
of 
the 
management capacity 
of 
the 
National 
Education 
System 
(SEN), 
and 
through 
the 
composition 
and 
generalization 
of 
a 
curriculum, 
that 
will 
strengthen 
citizen 
participation. 
This 
program 
has 
a strong 
impact 
on 
the 
development 
of 
human 
capital 
over 
the 
medium 
and 
long 
term. 
The 
poorest 
populations 
in 
the 
urban 
and 
peri-urban 
areas 
is 
the 
most 
impacted 
since 
they 
do not 
have 
as 
much 
access 
to 
private 
education 
(i.e., 
provided 
by 
mission 
church-subsidized 
schools) 
as 
the 
rural 
poor.

Page  86
12.

final

(ERP)

by

15. 
Budget 
for 
education 
quality 
improvement 
program. 
This 
program 
complements 
the 
activities 
of 
the 
Education 
Reform 
Program. 
It 
will 
finance 
the 
national 
counterpart 
for 
school construction 
and 
for 
selected 
pedagogic 
training 
and other 
activities 
supported 
by 
municipal 
governments. 
The operation 
of 
this 
program,  US$18.8 
million 
for 
2003, 
constitutes 
30 
percent 
of 
the 
total 
budget 
executed 
by 
the 
national 
social 
productivity 
fund 
(FPS) 
and 
is 
financed 78 
percent 
from 
external 
sources. 
By 
December 
31, 
2003, the 
program 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
executed 
in 
100 
percent, 
as 
this 
year’s 
budget 
is 
substantially 
less 
than last 
year’s 
execution 
(US$22.7 
million) 
and 
many 
of 
this 
year’s 
subprojects 
are 
already  under 
implementation. 
The 
expected 
results 
are 
917 
projects 
at 
the 
level 
of 
nuclear 
schools, 
consisting 
of 
563 subprojects 
for 
school infrastructure, 
299 
for 
training, 
37 
for 
equipment and 
18 
for 
pre-investment 
studies. 
16. 
Strategic 
sectoral 
planning. 
The 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
is 
preparing 
the 
Strategic 
Sectoral Plan. 
Some 
actions 
identified 
in 
the 
preliminary 
proposal 
to 
accelerate 
progress 
in 
reaching 
the 
goal 
of 
universal 
primary 
education 
include 
increasing 
the 
hours 
dedicated 
to 
learning, the 
substitution 
of 
non-trained 
teachers 
with 
professional 
teachers, 
and 
demand-side 
grants 
focused 
on 
poor  families 
in 
urban 
areas 
to 
avoid 
children 
dropping-out 
of 
school. 
Implementing 
the 
plan 
implies 
not 
only 
increased 
public 
investment 
but, 
especially, 
an increase 
in 
current 
expenditure. 
Financial 
sustainability 
is 
a 
concern 
that 
is 
currently 
being 
addressed 
by 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance 
and 
UDAPE, 
the 
social 
policy 
research agency 
of 
the government. 
17. 
For 
the 
release 
of 
the 
first 
tranche 
of 
the 
credit, 
a 
timetable 
must 
be

to

for

members 
of

society) 
on 
the

proposal 
for 
developing 
the 
PES. 
For 
the 
release 
of 
the 
second 
tranche 
of 
the 
credit,  the 
draft 
PES 
must 
have 
been 
submitted 
for

of 
the 
National 
Congress 
for

18. 
Options 
for 
school-age 
children at 
risk. 
To 
begin 
to 
address 
the 
deterioration 
in 
school 
attendance 
of 
children 
from 
the 
poorest 
families, 
the 
Social 
Safety 
Net 
Structural 
Adjustment 
Credit 
requires 
that 
further 
steps 
be taken 
to 
analyze 
the emergent trends and 
to 
plan 
appropriate 
responses. 
This 
will 
involve 
the 
identification 
of 
high-risk 
populations,  especially 
the 
poorest 
children 
at 
the 
primary 
level 
and 
the 
working 
teenagers 
at 
the secondary 
level. 
For 
the release 
of 
the 
first 
tranche 
of 
the credit, terms 
of 
reference 
of 
an 
empirical 
study 
on 
school 
attendance 
must 
be 
presented 
to 
the 
Bank. 
For 
the release 
of 
the 
second 
tranche,  a 
report 
on 
the 
findings 
and 
policy 
recommendations 
for 
targeted 
interventions 
identified 
for 
reversing 
the 
downward 
trend 
must be 
presented 
to 
the 
Bank.
